# UICare: Project Genesis - Phase Separation Board

---

## Phase 1: Core PRD Builder (Ideation Mode MVP)

**Goal:** Build a world-class early-stage SDL planning tool that can stand alone.

### Core Screens
- **Dashboard:** Manage active drafts, start new projects.
- **New Project Wizard:** Guided flow to input essential project info.
- **PRD Document Workspace:** Modular, editable PRD builder.
- **Export Center:** Export PRDs to Markdown, DOCX, GitHub repo-ready.
- **Settings:** User profile, integrations. (Buddy system prep hidden.)

### Key Features
- Modular Section Editing (drag/drop)
- Professional PRD Generation (Claude 3 / GPT-4o level quality)
- MVP Quick Start Templates
- Auto-Save Drafts (real-time local-first)
- WCAG 2.2+ Accessibility Compliance

---

## Phase 2: UICare Enhancement (Intervention Mode)

**Goal:** Protect creators under cognitive duress through invisible, trauma-informed systems.

### Cognitive Monitoring (Always-On)
- Typing cadence, task-switching, error looping detection
- Silent, dignified background monitoring during all user sessions

### Intervention Triggers
- Distress state detected (2+ indicators)
- Manual override option by user

### Rescue Agent System
- Project Memory Curator Agent
- 3 Parallel MVP Architect Agents
- Risk Assessor Agent
- Design Synthesizer Agent
- Buddy Comms Agent
- User Decision Facilitator Agent

### Buddy System
- MS Teams private channel creation
- Honor Code agreement workflow
- Intelligent Buddy Response Prompts

---

## Development Timeline for Hackathon

- **Day 1:** Dashboard + New Project Wizard (working stubs)
- **Day 2:** PRD Document Workspace (modular sections, autosave)
- **Day 3:** Export Center + core data persistence
- **Day 4:** Polish UX, add minimum Monitoring Layer hooks (no full agent system yet)

*UICare Intervention full build starts immediately after Hackathon for production readiness.*

# UICare Technical Architecture and Build Plan

## Project Overview and Goals
UICare is a novel AI-powered system designed to assist users (especially neurodivergent individuals such as those with bipolar disorder or autism) in completing projects when they become cognitively “stuck” or overwhelmed. The goal is to **monitor the user’s workflow**, detect when the user is trapped in an obsessive loop or manic state, and then **intervene with a team of AI agents** that help rescue the project. In essence, UICare will **prevent self-sabotage** by autonomously assembling the user’s recent work into a viable solution or MVP (Minimum Viable Product) and presenting a few alternative approaches to get the user back on track. 

Key objectives include: 
- **Phase 1 – Core PRD Builder:** Provide an AI agent that can gather information about the user’s project and generate a comprehensive Product Requirements Document (PRD). This helps define the project’s objectives and features clearly, which both guides the user and serves as a reference for the AI agents in Phase 2.
- **Phase 2 – UICare Intervention System:** Implement real-time cognitive state monitoring and a “rescue” mechanism. When the system detects the user is stuck in a destructive loop (e.g. obsessing over minor details or undoing progress), it triggers a coordinated multi-agent intervention. The agents will review recent work (last few days of code, documents, notes), clarify the project goals (using the PRD and/or by conversing with the user), and **autonomously produce 2-3 draft solutions or MVP versions** of the project. These are then presented to the user to help them regain momentum.

This ambitious project is aimed at **boosting productivity and mental well-being** by offloading the heavy lifting to AI when the user is in distress. The system must be **polished, professional, and extensible** – far beyond a quick MVP – because it will handle complex, multi-step tasks and interact with potentially sensitive user data. 

## Alignment with AI Agents Hackathon Requirements
UICare’s design and tech stack are carefully chosen to align with the Microsoft **AI Agents Hackathon 2025** rules and goals. The hackathon emphasizes building innovative AI agents and showcases modern frameworks and Azure services【2†L58-L66】. Our plan directly addresses these criteria in several ways:

- **Agentic Framework Usage:** We leverage a multi-agent architecture for problem-solving, which is a core theme of this hackathon. By composing multiple AI agents that converse and collaborate to accomplish tasks, we demonstrate the power of an “agentic” approach【10†L92-L100】. (Microsoft’s AutoGen framework exemplifies this by enabling agents that chat and coordinate tasks autonomously【10†L92-L100】.) UICare will use similar concepts, either via a Node.js library or a Python service (e.g. Microsoft’s **Autogen** or **Semantic Kernel**) to orchestrate agent conversations. This directly answers the hackathon question of *“How did an agentic framework help you build this solution?”* – our solution is fundamentally built on agent cooperation.
- **Innovation and Impact:** The idea of AI agents rescuing a user’s project during a manic episode is unique and impactful. It addresses a real human problem with a creative application of AI. This level of innovation is a key judging criterion (Innovation is 20% of judging【7†L1-L4】).
- **Use of Microsoft Technologies:** Where possible, we integrate Microsoft AI services to align with hackathon categories. For instance, we plan to use **Azure OpenAI Service** (GPT-4 models) as the LLM backend for our agents, ensuring enterprise-grade reliability and compliance. We are also considering the new **Azure AI Agents Service** for hosting our agent orchestrator, or using **Microsoft 365 Copilot/Agents SDK** if integration with tools like Microsoft Teams or Outlook becomes relevant【6†L49-L57】. This would position us well for categories such as *“Best Azure AI Agent Service Usage”* or *“Best Copilot Agent”*, in addition to the *“Best Agent in JavaScript/TypeScript”* category (since our front-end and possibly back-end are in TypeScript)【6†L61-L69】.
- **Quality and Completeness:** The hackathon runs from April 8 to April 30, 2025【5†L1-L4】, and UICare’s plan is structured to deliver a complete, working prototype by the deadline. Both Phase 1 and Phase 2 will be implemented, demonstrating a full end-to-end scenario of an agent-assisted PRD builder and an intervention/rescue system. We avoid “rush MVP” shortcuts – instead of a flimsy demo, we aim for production-quality code, modular design, and a polished UX. This thoroughness will show judges that our solution isn’t just a concept but a viable product.

By meeting these requirements, UICare will not only be a strong hackathon submission but also lay the foundation for a real-world application beyond the event.

## Technology Stack and Overall Architecture
**Frontend:** We use **Next.js 14** (with the App Router) for the web application, alongside **TypeScript** for type-safe development. Next.js 14 is a modern React framework that brings performance improvements and a robust structure for building scalable apps【21†L24-L33】. The App Router in Next.js allows us to use **React Server Components and nested layouts** for better performance and organization, and it supports new features like Server Actions for seamless server mutations【22†L1-L9】【22†L25-L33】. The UI is styled with **Tailwind CSS v4** and pre-built components from **Shadcn UI**. Tailwind CSS v4 offers a high-performance, utility-first styling approach, with its new Rust-based engine (“Oxide”) making builds up to 10× faster than before【14†L75-L84】. Shadcn UI provides a set of **beautifully designed, accessible components** that we can easily integrate and customize in our Next.js app【17†L12-L19】. This combination lets us build a polished interface rapidly without sacrificing quality or consistency.

**Backend:** The backend consists of an **AI Agent Orchestration Service** and supporting APIs. We plan to implement this as a **Node.js (TypeScript) service** running within the Next.js application (via API routes or Server Actions) for simplicity during the hackathon, but with a clear separation of concerns so it could be split into independent microservices later for scalability. In some parts, we may also incorporate a **Python microservice** using Microsoft’s AutoGen framework to manage complex multi-agent conversations, since AutoGen is a powerful tool for multi-agent chat orchestration【10†L92-L100】. This would run as a separate service communicating with the Next.js backend via HTTP or WebSocket. (For example, Next.js could send a request to a Python API to initiate the agent conversation, and receive streamed responses.)

**Data Storage and State:** UICare will maintain data about the user’s project and recent work. We will use a **database** (likely PostgreSQL via Prisma ORM, or a cloud service like Azure Cosmos DB for JSON/document storage) to store:
- User profiles and authentication (if multi-user support is enabled).
- Project data: PRDs, documents, code snippets, and metadata from the past few days of work.
- Logs of agent interactions (for debugging and learning patterns).
We will also use in-memory caches and perhaps a **vector store** (such as Pinecone or Azure Cognitive Search with embeddings) to enable semantic search through the user’s recent notes/code. This helps agents **retrieve relevant context quickly** during the intervention (retrieval-augmented generation).

**Overall Architecture:** The system is structured in a **modular client-server architecture**:
- The **Next.js client** (running in the browser) provides the interactive UI. It handles real-time user input (e.g. the user typing or coding in an editor) and displays agent outputs or suggestions in real-time.
- The **Next.js server (Node)** provides server-side rendered pages and API endpoints. It will host route handlers for events like “generate PRD” or “trigger rescue”. These handlers will call into our agent modules or the external Python service.
- The **Agent Orchestrator** (which can be a module or separate service) is the brain of Phase 2. It manages multiple agent instances (or prompt contexts) and tools. For example, it might create an agent for searching the web, another for analyzing code, and others for generating solutions. The orchestrator ensures these agents work together and keeps track of the overall task state.
- Optionally, a **Real-time Collaboration Service** (e.g. a WebSocket server or use of something like Azure SignalR or Supabase Realtime) will push updates to the client. This is used to stream agent responses and enable multi-user collaboration (Phase 2 and editing features).

All components are containerized (Docker) for easy deployment. In production, we could deploy the Next.js app and the agent service on Azure (e.g. Azure App Service or Azure Container Apps), using environment-specific configuration to scale out as needed. 

Below is a high-level breakdown of major components in the architecture:

- **Next.js Frontend** (React, TypeScript):
  - Pages (App Router) for dashboard, project workspace, PRD view, etc.
  - Reusable UI components (from Shadcn UI and custom ones like text editors, chat windows).
  - State management for real-time updates (possibly using React Context or Zustand for local state, and Yjs or similar for shared collaborative state).
  - TailwindCSS for styling, ensuring a consistent design system.

- **Backend API & Agents (Node.js)**:
  - API Routes or Server Actions for:
    - Authenticating users and managing sessions.
    - Accepting a request to generate a PRD (Phase 1).
    - Starting an intervention (Phase 2 trigger).
    - Streaming agent conversation results back to client.
  - Agent Orchestration Module:
    - Defines agent roles, loads AI models (via Azure OpenAI SDK or OpenAI API).
    - Manages the lifecycle of an intervention: monitoring, triggering agents, aggregating results.
    - Integrates tools (web search, code execution sandbox, etc.) that agents can use.
    - Maintains memory of conversation or context (possibly using a vector store or in-memory).
  - If using Python AutoGen service:
    - A FastAPI or Flask app exposing endpoints to initiate multi-agent chat, using AutoGen’s `AgentChat` to spin up conversable agents【10†L92-L100】 and handle their dialogue. This service would encapsulate complex multi-agent logic so the Node backend can simply request “run rescue with context X” and get back results.

- **Database & Storage**:
  - PostgreSQL (or Azure Cosmos DB) for structured data (projects, PRDs, user accounts).
  - Blob storage (Azure Blob or AWS S3) if we need to save large artifacts (e.g. file versions, or if the agents produce actual code files).
  - Vector DB or embedding index for semantic lookup of past content (improves agents’ ability to recall relevant details from yesterday’s work, etc.).

This stack ensures we meet the scalability and quality goals. Next.js 14 and Tailwind v4 give us a fast, modern front-end. A Node/TS backend keeps the stack consistent for faster development, while still allowing integration of specialized Python frameworks if needed for agent logic. The design is cloud-ready and can scale: we can run multiple instances of the agent service behind a load balancer, and the database can be a managed cloud DB for reliability.

## Frontend Architecture (Next.js 14 + Tailwind UI)
The frontend will be a **single-page application** feel, built with Next.js’s App Router for a clean structure. We will partition the UI into logical sections and take advantage of Next.js features like layouts and server components for performance:

- **App Router Structure:** We will create an `app/` directory with nested routes. For example:
  - `/app/(dashboard)/dashboard/page.tsx` – A dashboard page listing the user’s projects and showing overall status (e.g., which projects have active agent interventions or upcoming deadlines).
  - `/app/(project)/project/[projectId]/page.tsx` – The main workspace for a project. This page will load the project’s PRD and editing interface. We might use a nested layout here: a sidebar for navigation (project files, PRD, settings) and a main content area for the editor or PRD viewer.
  - `/app/(auth)/login/page.tsx` – (If needed) Authentication pages. We plan to secure the app so only the user (or invited collaborators) can access their project data. Using NextAuth or a custom Azure AD auth can be integrated; Next.js 14 supports NextAuth beta which works with App Router【20†L9-L17】.
  - API route handlers under `app/api/` for backend actions (though we might also use Server Actions for form submissions in PRD builder to simplify).

  The App Router allows us to easily manage these routes and even do partial prerendering for faster loads. For example, we can prerender the dashboard and then stream in dynamic content like agent status as it loads, using Next.js 14’s Partial Prerendering feature【21†L31-L39】.

- **UI Components with Shadcn UI:** Rather than building all UI elements from scratch, we will utilize **shadcn/ui** components which are open-source, accessible React components styled with Tailwind. These include form inputs, modals, dropdowns, navigation menu, avatars, etc., which will ensure a consistent look and feel. We will customize them via Tailwind theme (shadcn provides a baseline design which we can adjust to match UICare’s branding). For example:
  - Use a Shadcn **Navbar** and **Sidebar** component for the app’s navigation structure.
  - Use Shadcn **Dialog/Modal** for things like confirming an intervention or displaying the 3 solution options the agents produce.
  - Use Shadcn **Tabs or Accordion** to organize content in the PRD (e.g., separate sections like “Overview”, “Features”, “Timeline”).
  - Use their **Toast/Notification** component to show alerts (e.g., “Intervention triggered” or “Agent suggestions ready”).

- **Custom Components:** In addition to the library components, we will create some custom ones crucial to this app:
  - **Collaborative Editor**: An editor area where the user can write project content. This could be a rich-text editor if the user is writing documents, or a code editor if working on code. We might integrate **Monaco Editor** (the engine behind VS Code) for a code editing experience inside the browser. Monaco can be embedded in React and supports TypeScript/JS well. If the user writes content (like a document or notes), a simpler Markdown editor might be used. This editor will emit events as the user types or makes changes – which our monitoring logic can use to detect loops (more on that in monitoring).
  - **Agent Chat Panel**: A sidebar or panel where the user can see messages from agents. For instance, if during the rescue the agents need to ask a question (“Could you clarify what the main goal of this module is?”), it would appear in this chat panel. The user can respond in real-time. This is essentially a chat UI (list of messages, input box). We can style it similar to a chatGPT interface using Tailwind (speech bubble style messages).
  - **PRD Display**: A viewer for the generated Product Requirements Document. This could be a simple read-only view that formats the PRD (which might be in Markdown or JSON). We’ll ensure it’s nicely styled (using typography utilities from Tailwind and perhaps the Shadcn **Card** or **Sheet** component to contain it).

- **Styling and Theming:** Tailwind CSS v4 will be used throughout for rapid UI development. We will configure a custom color palette that gives UICare a distinct, professional look (for example, calming blues or greens to fit the “care” theme, avoiding overly harsh colors to not overstimulate a user in a manic state). Tailwind’s utility classes allow us to ensure responsive design out of the box, so the app will be usable on different screen sizes (though primarily desktop for coding). Tailwind v4’s performance improvements (like its Rust engine) mean we can scale our CSS without build slowdowns【14†L75-L84】. We’ll also use Tailwind’s **Dark mode** support if we anticipate users coding at night (important for user comfort).

- **Client-Side State Management:** We will keep most state server-driven (via Next’s server components or API calls) to keep things consistent. But for interactive features like the editor content or live typing indicators, we will use a state management solution. **React Context** or **Zustand** (a lightweight state library) can hold the current project data and any UI state (like which agent panel is open). Collaboration data (like multiple cursors if collaborators join) might be handled by a CRDT solution (discussed below in Real-Time Collaboration). 

- **Performance and UX:** We aim for a smooth UX:
  - Use **Loading indicators** and skeletons while the AI is thinking (Next.js streaming can let us show partial UI and then fill in agent results as they come).
  - Ensure the app is **accessible** (ARIA labels, keyboard navigation) — Shadcn components help with accessibility out of the box.
  - Implement **optimistic UI updates** for minor actions. For example, if the user requests a PRD generation, we immediately show a “Drafting PRD...” message in the UI while the agent works.
  - Utilize Next.js dynamic import and code splitting to keep initial loads fast (e.g., load heavy components like the code editor only when the user navigates to the project page).

Overall, the frontend is designed to be **modern, responsive, and user-friendly**. It’s not just an admin panel for agents; it’s a supportive environment for a stressed user. That means the UI should remain uncluttered and clear. We’ll guide the user with tooltips or guide modals especially on first use (e.g., explaining how the intervention works, to avoid surprise). High code quality is maintained by using TypeScript for all React components, and we’ll enforce linting (ESLint) and formatting (Prettier) to ensure consistency.

## Backend & AI Agent Architecture
The backend of UICare is where the core intelligence resides. It is responsible for monitoring user activity, orchestrating multiple AI agents, and integrating any external tools or services needed for the agents to perform their tasks. We emphasize a **modular, scalable design** so that each piece (monitoring, agents, tools, etc.) is loosely coupled and can be improved independently.

### Cognitive Monitoring System
In order to know *when* to initiate the rescue, UICare will include a **Cognitive Monitoring System** that runs continuously (or periodically) while the user works:

- **Monitoring Signals:** We will capture various signals from the user’s interactions in the frontend. For example:
  - **Editing Patterns:** The system can track how the content in the editor changes over time. If we detect the user has been editing the same small section repeatedly without moving forward, or is rapidly making and undoing changes, these could be red flags. Concretely, we might measure “net progress” over time – e.g., the number of characters added vs removed in the past hour. If the user has a high ratio of removals or is oscillating around the same word count, it implies being stuck.
  - **Time and Focus:** If a user spends an unusually long time (say 30+ minutes) without completing a logical chunk of work (like not finishing a paragraph or a function) this might trigger a check. Also if near a known deadline (perhaps a due date stored in project data), the system can be extra vigilant.
  - **User Behaviors:** Provide a manual “Help Me” button as well – the user can self-report when they feel stuck or notice they are spiraling. This button immediately triggers the intervention, overriding other conditions. This is important as a fallback if automatic detection isn’t perfect.
  - In future, we could integrate more advanced signals (like sentiment analysis on the user’s comments or even physiological data from wearables), but for now we’ll focus on in-app behavior signals.

- **Detection Logic:** A background process (maybe implemented with a simple Node interval timer or a dedicated worker thread) will evaluate these signals against criteria for mania/loop detection. Initially, we’ll implement a **heuristic algorithm** combining the signals:
  - For example: “If in the last 20 minutes, less than 50 new characters were added to the document, AND at least 5 edit/undo cycles occurred on the same section, THEN likely stuck.”
  - Another rule: “If user deletes a large portion of content that they previously worked on for hours (potential self-sabotage), THEN alert.”
  We’ll fine-tune these rules with testing. The system will avoid false positives as much as possible; perhaps it will issue a gentle prompt “Are you feeling stuck? Need assistance?” to the user for confirmation before launching the full rescue, unless it’s a clear emergency scenario (like user wiping a whole file).

- **Agent Trigger:** Once the monitoring system determines an intervention is needed (or the user presses the help button), it triggers the **Rescue Orchestrator** (described below). This could be done by simply calling the orchestrator function within the backend, or by enqueueing a task in a job queue (for scalability). Using a queue (like Azure Service Bus or a simple in-memory queue) would allow the heavy work to be done asynchronously, ensuring the frontend remains responsive and our monitoring can continue in parallel. For the hackathon scope, a direct call is fine, but we design with a queue in mind for production.

- **State Snapshot:** Before the orchestrator proceeds, the system will take a “snapshot” of the user’s current work state. This means saving the current editor content (or latest code) as a version. This is a safeguard – if the user’s trance leads them to further modify or damage the project while the agents are working, we have a preserved copy of the last good state. It can also be part of the context the agents use (the snapshot plus maybe one or two recent versions give context of the trajectory of the work).

The cognitive monitoring component thus acts as the **guardian angel** that knows when to call in reinforcements. It’s designed to be extensible – new rules or even ML-based detection models can be plugged in later. We’ll keep this logic in a dedicated module (`monitor.ts`) for clarity.

### Multi-Agent Orchestration Design
Once an intervention is triggered, the **Agent Orchestrator** takes over. This orchestrator is essentially a controller that will initialize several AI agents (with distinct roles) and manage a conversation or workflow between them to produce the desired outcomes (the project solutions). 

We will implement the orchestrator in a flexible way, possibly inspired by frameworks like AutoGen or Semantic Kernel:
- In Node.js (TypeScript), we might implement a simple orchestrator class that uses the OpenAI API to create multiple chat agents. Alternatively, if using Python + AutoGen, we will define multiple `AssistantAgent` instances and a `UserProxyAgent` to represent the user, letting them converse【10†L110-L117】【10†L123-L130】. For the sake of the plan, we’ll describe it generally:

**Agent Roles:** We propose the following specialized agents in the rescue team:
1. **Context Analyst Agent:** This agent’s job is to gather and summarize all relevant context about the project. It will:
   - Collect the recent artifacts: code files, documents, notes from the last few days (which the orchestrator can supply from the database).
   - Read the PRD (if the PRD exists from Phase 1, this is a crucial input).
   - Perhaps fetch any external info: for instance, do a quick web search if the project involves a domain the user looked up (e.g., if building a machine learning model, maybe search for similar open-source projects).
   - Summarize the current state of the project and clarify what appears to be the remaining objectives. Essentially, it formulates “What was the user trying to achieve?” in a concise form. This summary can be reviewed by other agents or even sent to the user to confirm.
   - Tools: This agent might use a **Web Search Tool** (via Bing Search API) if needed to “research the product itself” as the user suggested. Integrating a search step ensures the PRD or final plan is informed by best practices or examples from the internet.

2. **Planner Agent (Project Manager):** Using the context summary, this agent breaks down the problem into solvable tasks. It behaves like a project manager or tech lead. It might say: “To get an MVP, we need to complete A, B, and C. Here’s the plan...”. This agent could output a structured plan or even pseudo-code outline for the solution. (If the PRD from Phase 1 is detailed, this agent might not be needed as much, because the PRD itself is a plan. But if the PRD was not up-to-date, the planner can refine it.)

3. **Solution Builder Agents:** We will have multiple agents of this type – say three – tasked with creating actual solution drafts. They will work in parallel, each given the same context (project info, PRD, plan) but with a twist:
   - **Solution Agent 1:** instructed to take a conservative, minimal-change approach (e.g., finish the project using mostly what’s already there).
   - **Solution Agent 2:** instructed to be creative or ambitious – maybe a radical refactor or a different angle to meet the objectives.
   - **Solution Agent 3:** instructed to try something intermediate or alternative. 
   
   By setting different system prompts or using different randomness (temperature settings), we encourage diverse outputs. The concept is to mimic having 3 independent experts tackling the problem, yielding the “three options” for the user. Multi-agent research shows that a team of agents can outperform single agents on complex tasks by bringing diverse perspectives【25†L7-L15】.

   Each Solution Builder will produce an output – this could be code, a written document, or a mix (depending on the project). For example, if the project is writing an essay, each agent writes a draft. If it’s coding, they might generate code snippets or even full code files. (We will likely have them produce something like a structured text that our frontend can display nicely – e.g., markdown with code blocks.)

4. **Critic/Evaluator Agent:** After the solutions are generated, we can optionally have a fourth agent act as a *critic* or *QA tester*. This agent reviews the three solutions and provides an assessment: it might point out potential flaws or advantages of each solution. This can help the user in deciding. For example, the critic might say “Option 1 is very close to your original work but might not fix the underlying bug. Option 2 meets the requirements but introduces a different style. Option 3 is the simplest and most tested approach.” The critic agent essentially performs a comparative analysis.

5. **User Proxy / Coordinator Agent:** In frameworks like AutoGen, a UserProxyAgent can represent the user and their instructions in the multi-agent conversation【10†L123-L130】. In our case, the orchestrator itself can serve this role by injecting the user’s needs as a system prompt to all agents (like “The user’s goal is X. You are team members collaborating to help achieve it.”). If needed, this coordinator agent can also relay questions from agents to the actual user. For instance, if a Solution Builder agent says “I need clarification on what the ‘analytics module’ should do,” the orchestrator can forward that to the frontend, which then asks the user in the chat panel. The user’s answer is then given back to the agents so they can continue. This human-in-the-loop capability is important – even though agents are autonomous, the user can always intervene or provide guidance, which is supported by our real-time collab interface.

**Inter-Agent Communication:** The agents will communicate via a conversation loop managed by the orchestrator. We can implement a simple protocol such as:
- Orchestrator gives each agent an initial prompt with their role and the context.
- Agents produce outputs (could be done in parallel for the independent ones, or sequentially if some depend on others).
- If sequential: ContextAgent might output summary -> Planner uses summary to output plan -> then all Solution agents take plan and work -> Critic takes all solutions and evaluates.
- The orchestrator gathers the results. It might also allow some back-and-forth: e.g., after each solution agent produces a draft, we could have a short round where the solution agents or the critic can suggest improvements (“Agent1, maybe you missed requirement X”). However, given time constraints of a hackathon, we might keep it to one iteration to start with.

We will implement guardrails in this communication:
- Timeouts or step limits to ensure the agents don’t loop endlessly.
- Prompts that constrain the agents to **stay on task and not go rogue** (e.g., system prompt: “Do not deviate from the project goal. Do not do anything destructive like deleting content; your job is only to propose solutions.”).
- Size limits on outputs to avoid huge, unwieldy results (though GPT-4 can handle large context, we should still focus them).

**AI Models:** For all these agents, the underlying model will likely be **GPT-4** (or GPT-3.5 for some less critical tasks if we want to save tokens). GPT-4’s larger context window (up to 32k tokens) is extremely helpful here, as we can feed in a lot of project context (PRD, code, notes)【10†L92-L100】. If using Azure OpenAI, we’ll use the `gpt-4-32k` model deployment. For the solution agents, we might even instruct one of them to use a coding model (like Codex or GPT-4 with a code focus) if the project is code-heavy, to see if it yields better code.

**Tool Integration:** Agents may use tools for specific needs:
- The Context Analyst could use a **Web Search API**. We can implement a tool that when invoked, performs a Bing search and returns snippets. The agent’s prompt can include instructions like: “If you need more information about X, you can ask for a `SEARCH(X)`.” Our orchestrator would detect that and fulfill it, then feed results back into the agent’s context.
- A **Code Execution Sandbox** might be used if we want an agent to run some code tests. For example, if the project is software and the solution agent writes code, we could allow an agent to run a small test suite or at least a syntax check. This could be done with a Node VM or a secure container that executes the code. Given hackathon time, this is an optional stretch goal. Initially, the critic agent can do a pseudo-evaluation by reasoning (e.g., “This code looks correct and matches requirements” without actual execution).
- **Memory/Knowledge Base:** Agents will have access to the project’s knowledge base (the PRD, previous drafts, etc.) possibly via a retrieval tool. We might vectorize all notes and let the agent query “What was said about feature ABC before?” and return a snippet.

All these pieces make the multi-agent system **seamless and collaborative**. Essentially, we’re orchestrating an **AI team meeting** where each member has a role, and together they output something that no single agent working alone could easily produce under such conditions【25†L7-L15】.

### Real-Time Collaboration and Communication
Real-time collaboration is an important aspect of UICare, both for multi-agent orchestration and for user-agent interaction (and potentially multi-user interaction). We incorporate features to support this:

- **Agent Result Streaming:** When the agents are generating content (especially the Solution Builders which might produce long outputs), we will stream the partial results to the frontend. The OpenAI API supports token streaming, and Next.js can forward that to the client via Server-Sent Events (SSE) or a WebSocket. We’ll likely implement an SSE endpoint: the frontend’s fetch call can handle a stream, appending tokens to the output as they arrive, giving the user a feeling of the text being typed out. This immediate feedback keeps the user engaged and reassured that something is happening (reducing anxiety during the wait).

- **Live Agent Chat:** As mentioned, if agents need to ask the user for clarification, we have a chat panel. This uses real-time messaging. We can implement this with WebSockets (for bi-directional comms). The backend orchestrator can send a message event like “Agent Alice asks: ‘What is the priority feature?’” which the frontend displays. The user can type an answer and send, which goes via WebSocket back to the orchestrator, which injects it into the agent conversation. This is essentially a **human-in-the-loop** real-time collaboration.

- **Collaborative Editing (Multi-user):** While the primary use-case is a single user with AI agents, we plan for extensibility where a colleague or coach could also join the session to help the user. For example, a friend might remotely monitor and intervene as well. To facilitate this, our architecture can support multiple client connections on the same project:
  - We’ll assign each project (or session) a unique ID, and use something like **Yjs (CRDT)** or operational transforms to sync the editor content among users. Yjs provides shared data types that sync automatically for collaborative editing【26†L5-L8】. We can integrate Yjs on the frontend, and use a WebSocket provider (or something like Supabase’s realtime channels or Liveblocks service) to broadcast updates. This will allow two users (or user + an assistant) to see each other’s changes live. 
  - For the scope of the hackathon, full collaborative editing may be a stretch goal, but we will implement at least the foundation: the data model supports multiple authors, and we test it with two browser sessions.
  - Additionally, the agent outputs can be treated as another “participant” in the collaborative space. For instance, when an agent provides a suggestion, it could automatically appear as a comment or suggestion in the document for the user to accept or reject (similar to Google Docs suggestions). This non-intrusive collaboration makes the AI feel like a helpful colleague rather than overwriting the user’s work without permission.

- **Concurrency and Consistency:** With real-time features, we have to manage consistency. We’ll utilize locks or version checks when the agent is integrating changes. For example, if the user continues typing while the agents are working, when the results come back we don’t want to overwrite new text. Possibly, we’ll present the agent’s solution in a separate pane rather than directly replacing anything, allowing the user to manually merge it. This way, there’s no conflict. If in future we auto-merge, we’d use diffing algorithms and maybe ask for user confirmation for destructive changes.

Technologies to implement real-time:
- **Next.js Middleware or custom server** for WebSockets: Since Next.js (especially on Vercel) doesn’t natively support persistent WebSocket easily, we might set up a small Node server (or use something like Socket.IO) alongside. Alternatively, use a service like **Ably or Azure Web PubSub** to handle the real-time channel. For hackathon simplicity, we can rely on SSE for one-way streaming and simulate two-way by long-poll or short polling for responses (though not ideal). A quick integration could be using **Supabase Realtime** which gives us a free WebSocket for Postgres changes: we could insert chat messages into a table and have both clients and server listen. However, that might be overkill. We will likely choose the path of least resistance given time, but ensure the architecture allows plugging a proper real-time service later.

In summary, the system treats the user and the agents as collaborators working together in real-time. This is a cutting-edge aspect of UICare, as many agent systems are offline or single-turn. We believe this interactive approach is necessary for a **highly responsive and user-friendly experience**.

## Phase 1: Core PRD Builder Implementation
Phase 1 focuses on building the **Core PRD Builder** – essentially an AI agent (or set of agents) that helps create a **Product Requirements Document** for the user’s project. The PRD is crucial because it serves as a roadmap and single source of truth about what the project is supposed to accomplish. It will later guide the rescue agents in Phase 2. Here’s how we will implement the PRD Builder:

- **User Input Gathering:** The PRD builder can be invoked either when a user starts a new project or on demand if they want to refine the requirements. We’ll provide a simple UI to kick it off, e.g., a form or wizard asking a few high-level questions:
  - “What is the goal of your project in one sentence?”
  - “Who is the target user or audience?”
  - “List any specific features or ideas you already have.”
  This gives the AI a starting point. The user can also upload any notes or draft they have made.

- **Agent Processing (PRD Drafting):** Once initiated, a dedicated **PRD Agent** will take the user input and possibly do some **research** before producing the PRD:
  - The agent will query external resources to “research the product itself” if relevant. For example, if the user says “I’m building a to-do list app,” the agent might quickly search for “essential features of a to-do list app”【2†L63-L66】 to ensure it doesn’t miss common requirements (like due dates, reminders, etc.). This is the *deep research* component that was emphasized – the agent doesn’t operate in a vacuum, it leverages existing knowledge of similar products.
  - The agent may use a combination of prompts and perhaps a tool: we can integrate the Bing search tool similar to Phase 2’s context agent. The findings from the search (like an article listing top 10 features of great to-do apps) will be fed into the prompt.
  - Next, the agent organizes the PRD content. A typical PRD includes:
    - **Project Overview:** summary of the idea and why it’s being built.
    - **Objectives and Success Criteria:** what constitutes success (e.g., “user can accomplish X”).
    - **Features List:** key features or requirements, often prioritized.
    - **Technical Approach:** (if applicable) any particular tech or design decisions.
    - **Timeline/Milestones:** a rough timeline or phases of implementation (for hackathon maybe not needed, but could include if known deadlines).
    - **Risks and Mitigations:** potential challenges.
    - We will instruct the agent to output the PRD in a structured markdown format with headings for each of these sections, for easy display.

- **Review and Edit:** The draft PRD will be presented in the UI. Because this is a collaborative process, the user should review it and make corrections or additions. The PRD Builder interface could allow the user to have a short back-and-forth with the agent: e.g., the user might say “I also want it to have a dark mode feature, please add that,” and the agent will insert that into the features section. This can be done via a chat-like interface specifically for PRD refinement, or simply the user edits the text manually – whichever is easier to implement. We might lean on manual editing for hackathon simplicity, but mention that the agent can be asked to regenerate sections if needed (“regenerate features list” button for instance).

- **Quality of PRD:** We will ensure the PRD agent uses high-quality prompts so that the output is **coherent, comprehensive, and correct**. The inclusion of external research helps ensure comprehensiveness (the agent might include best practices that the user didn’t mention). We will test the PRD agent with a few example project ideas to fine-tune its prompt before finalizing. The PRD is stored in the database for later use.

- **Integration with Phase 2:** Once the PRD is finalized (or even while it’s in draft), it becomes a reference artifact. The monitoring system and rescue agents will know to pull the latest PRD as part of context. We’ll likely maintain versioning – if the user updates the PRD later, we keep both versions or the latest, depending on what the agents need.

Phase 1 deliverable is that by the end of the first development phase, **UICare can generate a solid PRD for any given project idea with minimal input**. This not only is useful for our hackathon submission narrative (we can show “here’s how our system even helped define the project requirements”), but also gets the user thinking clearly about their project which might in itself reduce the chance of getting stuck.

## Phase 2: UICare Intervention System (Rescue Agents)
Phase 2 is the heart of UICare – the Intervention System that actively pulls the user out of a destructive loop and steers the project to safety. Building on the monitoring and multi-agent orchestration described earlier, here we outline the concrete implementation and flow of Phase 2:

1. **Detection & Trigger:** The monitoring system flags that an intervention is needed (automatically or via user request). Suppose our user has been tweaking the same paragraph for an hour or has started deleting large sections of code in a frenzy – the system recognizes this pattern and triggers Phase 2.

2. **Freeze & Snapshot:** Immediately, the system creates a snapshot of the current state (all relevant files, documents). It also may temporarily lock further edits from the user (maybe not hard lock, but perhaps show a prompt “Initiating rescue...”). We don’t want the user to keep thrashing while the rescue is happening, but we also understand they might not stop. A gentle approach is to *encourage* a break: e.g., show a calming message: “Hang tight, we’re analyzing your project and preparing some solutions for you. Take a deep breath – help is on the way!” Possibly even play a gentle animation or sound to signify it’s under control.

3. **Context Compilation:** The orchestrator gathers everything the agents need:
   - Last 2-3 days of project history from the database (could be commit messages, file diffs, or daily summaries if we stored).
   - The final PRD (from Phase 1).
   - The last known good state of the work (maybe a version from yesterday when not manic).
   - Any notes about what the user was trying to do next (maybe from a to-do list if the user maintained one).
   This is packaged into a context that will be fed into the agents.

4. **Agent Team Activation:** The orchestrator spins up the agents as per the design:
   - **ContextAgent** reads everything and produces the summary of “What’s the project about, what’s done, what seems to be missing.”
   - **Planner** (if used) outlines a plan to complete the project.
   - **Three Solution Agents** begin their work concurrently (since each is independent once they have context and plan).
     - We will run three separate OpenAI calls in parallel (Next.js can handle parallel API calls or we could use Promise.all in Node). Because each call could be lengthy (especially with GPT-4 on a big context), running concurrently saves time.
     - Each agent might take, say, 30-60 seconds to produce a non-trivial solution (just an estimate, given big context). We will stream their outputs as they come.
   - The orchestrator collects the outputs. If using a Python orchestrator (AutoGen), it might manage a conversation, but likely we’ll keep it simpler and orchestrate ourselves to ensure timely results.
   - (Optional) **Critic Agent** runs after all outputs are ready, taking a short time to analyze them. This could be done with a relatively small prompt (just feed summaries of each option and ask for comparison). We can display the critic’s notes alongside options as “AI feedback.”

5. **Presenting Solutions:** The frontend now needs to display the outcome to the user in a clear and calm manner:
   - We likely show a modal or a dedicated “Rescue Results” page that overlays the workspace.
   - It will have **three columns or sections**, one for each solution option. Each section has a title like “Option 1”, “Option 2”, “Option 3” and underneath, the content. If the content is text (for a written project), we show the text. If it’s code, we can show a formatted diff or code blocks. If it’s a mix (maybe the agent says “I created a prototype and here’s the summary”), we just present it as given.
   - We’ll include any summary from the Critic agent or key points to help evaluation. Perhaps a short bullet list under each option: e.g., “Pros: Minimal changes. Cons: Doesn’t add the new feature X.”
   - The UI could allow the user to click on an option to **accept it**. Accepting an option would do one of two things:
     - If it’s primarily text changes: we could replace the current content with the chosen option’s content.
     - If it’s code: we might create a new branch or version in the system with that code. For hackathon simplicity, perhaps we just provide a link to download the code or view it file by file. In a real version, we’d integrate with Git (e.g., push the three options as three git branches so the user can check them out).
   - The user might also decide to **not fully accept any single option** but take ideas from each. Our UI should support that: maybe allow copy-pasting bits, or just keep the suggestions view open while they manually incorporate changes in the editor. This is where having a side-by-side view or the ability to toggle an option into the editor for editing would be useful.

6. **Post-Intervention Merge:** After the user chooses an option (or finishes using the suggestions), the system should help merge it into the project:
   - We’ll treat the chosen solution as the new current state of the project in our database (with user approval). This could involve updating the PRD if the scope changed, or marking certain tasks as done.
   - We also log this intervention outcome for future learning (maybe the next time, the system can see what worked or if multiple interventions happen, we adjust thresholds).
   - If the user is on a deadline (as in the user story, “there’s a deadline next morning”), presumably after this intervention, they now have a near-finished product to polish. The system effectively helped them meet the deadline. They can then continue lightly editing if needed, hopefully out of the manic loop.

7. **Agent Shutdown and Hand-off:** Once done, the agents shut up 😄. We don’t want them to keep interfering after the rescue. The monitoring might take a break or raise the threshold for triggering again (so it doesn’t keep firing repeatedly the same night). The user is back in control, ideally calmer and with renewed focus thanks to seeing concrete progress.

Throughout this flow, we pay attention to the **user experience**: the intervention should feel like a helpful assist, not a takeover. That’s why we make it collaborative (asking questions, offering options). We avoid just automatically changing the user’s project without their input – that could cause anxiety or mistrust. Instead, we empower the user with suggestions and let them decide.

On a technical note, we will thoroughly test this phase with scenario simulations. For example, we’ll simulate a scenario with a dummy project (like writing an essay) where we intentionally create a “stuck” situation and see if the agents manage to produce meaningful output. We’ll refine prompts for each agent role accordingly. The result should demonstrate the full cognitive loop: detection → multi-agent collaboration → solution options.

## Codebase Structure and Modularity
To ensure code quality and maintainability, we will organize the repository into a clear structure with separation of concerns. Below is a proposed **directory structure** for the codebase:

```
/uicare-project
├── app/  (Next.js App Router pages and API routes)
│   ├── (dashboard)/dashboard/page.tsx        # Dashboard page
│   ├── (project)/project/[id]/page.tsx       # Project workspace page
│   ├── (project)/project/[id]/layout.tsx     # Layout for project pages (sidebar + main)
│   ├── (auth)/login/page.tsx                 # Login page (if auth enabled)
│   ├── api/
│   │   ├── generate-prd/route.ts            # API Route to trigger PRD generation
│   │   ├── intervention/route.ts           # API Route to trigger intervention
│   │   ├── agent-stream/[sessionId]/route.ts # (possibly) SSE stream for agent output
│   │   └── ... (other API endpoints as needed)
│   └── globals.css                          # Tailwind base styles, etc.
├── components/                              # Reusable UI components
│   ├── Editor.tsx            # Text/Code editor component
│   ├── AgentChatPanel.tsx    # UI for agent-user chat
│   ├── ProjectSidebar.tsx    # Sidebar showing project info, files, PRD link
│   ├── SolutionModal.tsx     # Modal dialog to display solution options
│   ├── Navbar.tsx            # Top navigation bar
│   └── ... (other UI pieces like buttons, form inputs if custom)
├── lib/                                     # Utility libraries (non-UI)
│   ├── agents/
│   │   ├── orchestrator.ts    # Main orchestrator logic for multi-agent coordination
│   │   ├── roles/
│   │   │   ├── contextAgentPrompt.ts   # Prompt templates or logic for Context Analyst
│   │   │   ├── plannerPrompt.ts        # Prompt for Planner agent
│   │   │   ├── solutionAgentPrompt.ts  # Prompt for Solution Builder (generic or 3 variants)
│   │   │   ├── criticPrompt.ts         # Prompt for Critic agent
│   │   │   └── ... (any other role-specific code)
│   │   ├── tools.ts          # Implementation of tools (search API calls, code exec)
│   │   ├── memory.ts         # Functions to retrieve project context (e.g., get recent notes, vector search)
│   │   └── agentUtils.ts     # Shared helper functions for agent interactions
│   ├── monitoring/
│   │   ├── monitorLoop.ts    # The cognitive monitoring loop logic
│   │   ├── heuristics.ts     # Functions to evaluate editing patterns
│   │   └── notifier.ts       # Maybe module to send trigger to orchestrator or notify user
│   ├── db.ts                 # Database client (Prisma or similar config)
│   ├── auth.ts               # Auth setup (if using NextAuth or custom)
│   └── utils.ts              # Misc utilities (e.g., formatting, logging)
├── public/                                  # Public assets (if any, e.g., images, icons)
├── styles/                                  # Additional styling (maybe Tailwind config etc.)
├── README.md
└── ... config files (tailwind.config.js, next.config.js, tsconfig.json, etc.)
```

**Modularity:** In this structure, front-end components are decoupled from backend logic:
- The `components/` folder has purely presentational/interactive components (React). They fetch or receive data via props or context, but they don’t contain business logic.
- The `lib/agents` and `lib/monitoring` contain the core logic for the AI and monitoring. This can be unit-tested independently. For example, we can test `heuristics.ts` functions by feeding sample edit histories and ensuring they return true/false appropriately for stuck scenarios. We can test `orchestrator.ts` with a mocked OpenAI API to ensure it orchestrates calls in the right order.
- The API routes (`app/api/...`) act as thin controllers that call into these lib functions. This separation means if later we split the backend out of Next.js, we could move these lib files into a standalone server with minimal changes.

**Shadcn UI integration:** Shadcn’s components often come as copied code into the project (since you can tailor them). We might place those in `components/ui/` subfolder for clarity, or keep them alongside our components. Each Shadcn component (like Dialog, Toast) will typically consist of a couple of files (e.g., `Dialog.tsx` with Tailwind classes and Radix primitives). We’ll ensure these are organized and perhaps documented so other devs know how to reuse them.

**State Management:** If we use any global state (for collab presence or such), we might have a context provider in `app/project/[id]/layout.tsx` that provides a state store to children. That might be defined in a `lib/state.ts` or within the component file itself using React Context API. We keep state minimal though, leveraging server-provided data whenever possible (Next can fetch initial data for the project via server components).

**Quality Practices:** 
- We will configure **TypeScript strict mode**, so types must be correct. This reduces runtime bugs.
- Use **ESLint** with an appropriate config (Next.js comes with a base, we add rules for code style and perhaps accessibility).
- Use **Prettier** for consistent formatting of code.
- Write **unit tests** for critical functions (if time permits during hackathon). At least, the architecture is testable: e.g., you could test `agents/memory.ts` functions by populating a test database and checking that context assembly works.

**Extensibility:** The codebase is organized by feature, so new features can be added logically. For instance, if later we add a “distraction notifier” (some feature outside of current scope), we can add a new module without touching existing ones, as long as it hooks into maybe the monitoring or frontend via a new component.

Finally, documentation: We will document each module with comments, and the README.md will contain an overview (some content from this plan) so that any new contributor (or hackathon judge) can navigate the code easily. The structured approach demonstrates that we haven’t thrown everything in one file; instead we have a clean **separation of front-end, back-end logic, agent prompts, and utilities**, which is hallmark of production-quality software.

## Quality Assurance and Testing
Even under hackathon time constraints, we strive for a high-quality, reliable application. Our QA approach includes:

- **Manual Testing of User Scenarios:** We will walk through the primary use cases end-to-end:
  1. **PRD Generation Flow:** Start a new project, input some idea description, run the PRD builder, and inspect the output. Verify the PRD covers key points and the format is correct. We’ll do this for a couple of project types (one software, one writing-focused, for example) to see how the agent adapts.
  2. **Monitoring Trigger:** Simulate a user editing in a way that should trigger intervention. For instance, in the dev environment, we might manually call the API or use a debug flag to force an intervention (since mimicking exact user keystrokes might be tricky). We ensure that when triggered, the intervention process starts and the UI shows the appropriate feedback (like the “rescue in progress” message).
  3. **Intervention Outcome:** Let the agent run (possibly on a smaller example to be quick) and ensure that the three options show up properly in the UI, and that selecting one does what we expect (replaces text, etc.). We also test if the user ignores the suggestions, that nothing breaks (they can continue editing, maybe the suggestions just stay until dismissed).
  4. **Agent Q&A Loop:** Test a scenario where agents ask the user a question. We can force this by providing incomplete info so the agent naturally queries (e.g., leave out a crucial requirement and see if agent asks). Verify the question appears in chat and that our answer goes back to agent and influences output.

- **Unit Tests for Logic:** Focus on the critical algorithms:
  - Monitoring heuristics: we can feed a mock sequence of edits (like an array of operations: add X chars, delete Y chars, etc.) to a function and assert whether it classifies “stuck” or not.
  - Simple prompt tests: While testing AI prompts is tricky (due to variability), we can at least test our formatting functions (e.g., if we have a function that truncates context to fit model limits, test that it works).
  - If we implement any parsing of agent outputs (for example, maybe the agent outputs JSON or markdown and we parse it), that parser should be tested on sample outputs.

- **Integration Testing (Lightweight):** We might write a script to simulate an entire intervention by calling our API routes in sequence, to ensure data flows correctly. For example, create a project in the test DB, call generate-prd, then simulate some user activity, then call intervention and see that results come back. Given time, this might be done manually, but having a script helps for regression if we change something last minute.

- **Performance Testing:** Given this is a hackathon, we do not expect heavy load (likely single-user usage during demo). However, we will check that our streaming doesn’t lag significantly and that agent responses come in a reasonable timeframe. If we find any bottleneck (like vector search too slow), we’ll optimize or stub it for now.

- **Error Handling:** We will implement robust error handling paths:
  - If the OpenAI API fails or times out, the user should get a clear message (“Sorry, something went wrong with the AI service. Please try again.”) rather than the app crashing. We’ll use try/catch around API calls and handle gracefully.
  - If the monitoring triggers erroneously, and the user cancels, ensure that doesn’t break anything (i.e., agents should stop if not needed).
  - Validate inputs: e.g., if user inputs nothing for PRD, agent should still handle it or prompt for more info.

- **Security Considerations:** Although not heavily in focus, we ensure that:
  - The user’s data is secure. If multi-user, ensure one user cannot access another’s project by changing URL (Next.js route will fetch data by user session; we’ll implement checks server-side).
  - API routes that perform actions are protected (maybe using an auth token or session).
  - Prevent prompt injection from user input: since the user’s own text goes into prompts, theoretically they could sabotage the agent. But since the user and agent are on the same side here, it’s less an issue. If we allowed any collaborator, we might sanitize inputs going into prompts.

- **Polish and UX Testing:** Check that the UI is polished:
  - Look for any Tailwind style issues on different screen sizes.
  - Ensure dark mode works if we provide it.
  - Test keyboard navigation (can user trigger the help button with a keyboard, etc., to adhere to accessibility).

Finally, we will do a **practice demo/run-through** exactly as we plan to present to hackathon judges. This way we can iron out any last glitches and also measure how long the agent processing takes to perhaps shorten for demo (maybe by using a smaller model or pre-setting some results if absolutely needed for demo smoothness, though that’s a last resort).

Our commitment is that even as a hackathon project, UICare will be delivered with a level of quality approaching a production application, thanks to these testing and QA measures.

## Timeline and Phased Execution Plan
To meet the April 30 hackathon deadline, we have structured the development into phases with clear milestones. Below is the timeline with key tasks and milestones aligned roughly by week (given a 3-week hackathon):

- **Week 1 (Apr 8 – Apr 14): Project Setup & Core Architecture**  
  - *Setup:* Initialize the Next.js 14 project with TypeScript. Set up Tailwind CSS v4 config and integrate shadcn UI library (pull in a few base components to verify styling). Initialize Git repository.
  - *Architecture Design:* Write a draft of the technical plan (largely what this document is) to solidify the approach. Review Microsoft AI Agents Hackathon rules and ensure our plan aligns (e.g., verify usage of Azure OpenAI is allowed, etc.). **Milestone:** Completed architectural plan and project scaffolding (empty pages, navigation placeholders).
  - *Basic Frontend:* Implement the main layout and navigation (dashboard and a dummy project page). Get authentication working if needed (could skip if not focusing on multi-user). Ensure styling is consistent.
  - *Backend groundwork:* Set up the database schema using Prisma (Project, User, PRD entities). Implement a simple API route to create a project and store an initial PRD (even if empty). Test database connectivity.
  - *Agent Integration Prep:* If using any SDK (OpenAI, Azure), install and configure environment (API keys, etc.). Perhaps run a quick test call to OpenAI API from the backend to ensure everything is wired.

- **Week 2 (Apr 15 – Apr 21): Implement Phase 1 and Core Systems**  
  - *PRD Builder Agent:* Develop the PRD generation functionality. Create prompt templates and logic for calling the OpenAI API. Integrate a search API if possible this week. **Milestone:** By mid-week, the PRD Builder should be able to take a sample input and return a structured PRD, viewable in the UI.
  - *UI for PRD Builder:* Build the form or interface to input project details and a page to display the PRD result. Make it look nice (use Shadcn components for the form and a good typography style for PRD).
  - *Cognitive Monitoring (initial):* Start coding the monitoring logic. Perhaps simulate it with a manual trigger first (button). Set up the structure for capturing editor events. By end of week, have a function that can analyze a made-up sequence for “stuck” behavior.
  - *Real-time Infrastructure:* If we decide on using SSE or WebSocket, implement a basic example: e.g., an API route that streams time ticks to the frontend to ensure our mechanism works. This lays groundwork for streaming agent responses later.
  - *Testing & Refinement:* Test PRD builder with team/friends and refine prompts. Make sure it’s reliable. Also ensure any library like Yjs or others are properly installed if we plan to use (maybe experiment with a small collaborative text example to see complexity).
  - **Milestone:** Phase 1 (PRD Builder) completed and integrated. You can create a project and get a PRD out. This can be shown as a mini-demo.

- **Week 3 (Apr 22 – Apr 28): Implement Phase 2 (Intervention System)**  
  - *Multi-Agent Orchestrator:* Implement the orchestrator and agent roles. This is a heavy week for backend. We might start with a simplified approach: e.g., initially just do one solution agent to get the pipeline working, then expand to three in parallel. Also implement the ContextAgent that reads from DB.
  - *Agent Conversations:* If using AutoGen (Python), set up the Python service this week and test a basic multi-agent conversation (maybe a trivial example first). Ensure communication between Node and Python (could be via HTTP calls).
  - *Monitoring Integration:* Tie the monitoring trigger to actually call the orchestrator. For now, maybe trigger on a simple condition or via a button press.
  - *Frontend for Intervention:* Develop the SolutionModal component or page to display results. Start by mocking three results to design the layout. Then hook it up to real outputs. Implement the streaming of agent outputs to this modal (likely using SSE – set up an `/api/agent-stream` that the orchestrator writes events to).
  - *Real-time Chat:* Implement the AgentChatPanel and the logic for user-agent Q&A. At minimum, get a message sending from user to backend and back. Time permitting, integrate this into the orchestrator loop.
  - *Testing Agents:* Throughout this week, test the multi-agent process with various scenarios (small test projects). Adjust prompts and fix any coordination issues (like agents talking past each other or irrelevant output).
  - **Milestone:** By April 28, the system should be capable of detecting a stuck scenario and producing at least one rescue solution end-to-end. Ideally all three options and the UI to select them is functional by this date, leaving a couple of days for polish.

- **Final Stretch (Apr 29 – Apr 30): Polishing and Submission Prep**  
  - *Polish UI/UX:* Refine styling, add instructions or help text where needed for clarity. Ensure mobile responsiveness (just in case).
  - *Bug Fixes:* Squash any critical bugs found in testing. Ensure the app doesn’t crash under normal use. If some features are flaky (e.g., multi-user collab), decide if to hide them for submission to avoid judge confusion.
  - *Performance Tuning:* If agent calls are too slow, see if we can optimize (maybe reduce context size slightly or use gpt-3.5 for less crucial parts). Ensure streaming is enabled for GPT-4 to not hit request timeouts.
  - *Prepare Demo Script:* Write a short script of how we will demo the project to judges. Possibly prepare a specific scenario to showcase (maybe pre-fill some data so it’s faster).
  - *README and Video:* According to hackathon submission, prepare the documentation. The README will include what the project does, how to run it, and how an agentic framework was used (we will mention how our multi-agent approach was built and possibly reference frameworks【10†L92-L100】). If a demo video is required, record a usage demonstration.
  - *Submission:* Package the project (e.g., push to GitHub) and submit via the hackathon portal by Apr 30, 11:59 PM PT.

Each phase is designed with a clear focus, but some tasks overlapped intentionally (for example, started monitoring in Week 2 while still finishing PRD builder). This overlapping ensures that by the final week we weren’t starting anything from scratch, just integrating and refining.

Given the ambitious scope, prioritization is key. Our must-have by submission is the **core scenario working** (monitor triggers > agents produce solutions > user sees and benefits). Nice-to-haves like multi-user collaboration or a super advanced critic agent can be trimmed if time is too short. However, our plan accounted for buffer in the final stretch to get things in a presentable, robust state.

## Conclusion
By following this comprehensive plan, we will build UICare into a **scalable, high-quality application** that not only meets the hackathon’s requirements but also provides genuine utility for users prone to getting stuck in their projects. We adhered to the Microsoft AI Agents Hackathon guidelines by leveraging cutting-edge AI agent frameworks and ensuring our design is innovative and agent-centric【2†L58-L66】【6†L49-L57】. The frontend stack (Next.js 14, TypeScript, TailwindCSS v4, Shadcn UI) gives us a strong foundation for a professional-grade user interface, while the backend’s multi-agent architecture provides the intelligence and orchestration necessary for cognitive monitoring and intervention. 

In summary, UICare’s build plan emphasizes:
- **Code Quality & Modularity:** Clear separation of components and logic, making the codebase maintainable and extensible (no throwaway MVP code).
- **Real-Time Collaboration:** The user remains in the loop with live updates and can work alongside the AI, rather than being replaced by it.
- **Seamless Agent Orchestration:** Multiple AI agents cooperate to solve complex tasks autonomously, exemplifying the promise of agentic AI in 2025【25†L7-L15】.
- **Phased Delivery:** Achieving a working PRD builder early, then layering on the more complex intervention system, to ensure incremental success.
- **Production Readiness:** Consideration for error handling, performance, and scalability (cloud deployment, Azure services) from the get-go, aligning with a vision beyond the hackathon.

With this plan executed, UICare will be a standout submission demonstrating how AI agents can augment human creativity and productivity in a caring and effective way. We are confident this architecture will yield a polished product by the deadline and serve as a foundation for future development of the UICare platform.

### 1. Overview  
Deliver a production-quality AI-driven system that  
- **Monitors** user workflow (detects “stuck” or manic loops)  
- **Generates** a detailed PRD (Phase 1)  
- **Triggers** a multi-agent “rescue” (Phase 2) producing 3 parallel solutions plus critique  
- **Streams** results in real time and preserves user control  

**Deadline:** April 30, 2025 (Hackathon rules compliant)  
**Stack (mandatory):** Next.js 14 App Router, TypeScript, Tailwind v4, Shadcn UI

| Real-Time Opt A | Server-Sent Events (SSE) for streaming                          |

  - `/app/(dashboard)/page.tsx` → Dashboard list

  - Server components for data fetch

- **Signals Captured**  
  - Edit pattern: add vs. delete ratio over time  
  - Edit/undo loops on same section  
  - Time spent without net progress  
  - Manual “Help Me” button  
- **Detection Rules (deterministic)**  
  1. If last 20 min: < 50 new chars **AND** ≥ 5 undo cycles on same loc → trigger  
  2. If user deletes > 50% content worked on > 1 hr → trigger  
- **Trigger Behaviour**  
  - Snapshot current state  
  - Optional gentle prompt: “Need assistance?”  
  - Direct call to orchestrator

   - Summarize last 2–3 days of code/docs + PRD

   - Break summary into structured actionable plan  
3. **Solution Builder Agents (3 parallel)**  
   - **Option 1:** Conservative (minimal changes)  
   - **Option 2:** Creative (alternative approach)  
   - **Option 3:** Balanced (mix of both)  
4. **Critic Agent**  
   - Comparative analysis of 3 solutions  
5. **Coordinator**  
   - Forwards user clarifications from chat panel into agent prompts  

**Model:** GPT-4-32k (Azure OpenAI) for all agents; GPT-3.5 for non-critical tasks

- Web Search (Bing)  
- Code sandbox (optional)  
- Vector retrieval for memory
