# Microsoft AI Agents Platform & GitHub Setup

## Azure AI Agents Service (Preview) for UICare  
Choosing **Azure AI Agent Service (Preview)** as the platform for UICare ensures alignment with Microsoft’s hackathon guidelines and takes advantage of cutting-edge AI capabilities. In fact, the hackathon explicitly encourages using this service – there’s even a dedicated prize category for the **“Best Azure AI Agent Service Usage”**【9†L255-L262】. Azure AI Agent Service is a fully managed platform for building autonomous AI agents, allowing developers to focus on agent logic without worrying about infrastructure【11†L73-L82】【10†L49-L57】. By using this official Microsoft service (currently in public preview), UICare can leverage pre-built integrations (e.g. code execution, web search, Azure integrations) while staying within the hackathon’s rules and technology focus.

## Setting Up the Azure AI Agent Workspace  
To implement UICare on Azure’s platform, you’ll create and configure an **Azure AI Agent Service workspace** (via the Azure AI Foundry portal). Below is a step-by-step guide to setting up the agent environment:

1. **Prerequisites – Azure Subscription and Foundry Hub:** Ensure you have an Azure subscription with proper permissions. If you don’t have an account, create a free Azure account (you get credits for trial use)【27†L62-L70】. You will need the Azure AI Developer role (or Contributor/Owner on a Resource Group) to create an **Azure AI Foundry Hub** – this hub is essentially the workspace container for your AI projects. In the Azure Portal, create a new *Azure AI Foundry* resource (formerly known as Azure AI Studio) to serve as your hub.

2. **Create an AI Project:** Within the Foundry hub, create a new **Project** (this will represent the UICare agent project). Give the project a meaningful name (e.g. “UICareAgent”). The project will encapsulate models, agents, and any linked resources. If prompted to select a hub or create a new one, choose your newly created hub【13†L85-L93】. Once the project is created, navigate into it (dismiss any intro tips).

3. **Deploy a Base Model:** Before creating an agent, deploy a language model in your project’s assets. In the project’s left menu, go to **My assets > Models + endpoints** (or a similar section for models). Click “+ Deploy model” and select a base model to deploy (for example, **gpt-4o-mini** or another GPT-4 variant)【27†L83-L90】. Choosing a smaller model like *gpt-4o-mini* is wise for development, as it conserves resources and stays within free tier quotas【27†L83-L90】. Confirm the model deployment – it may take a moment for Azure to spin up the model endpoint.

4. **Create the AI Agent:** Now configure the UICare agent itself. In the left-hand pane under **Build & Customize**, select **Agents**, then click **+ Create agent**【27†L98-L106】. In the *Agent Setup* dialog, provide:  
   - A **Name** for your agent (e.g., “UICareAssistant”).  
   - The **Model**: ensure you select the model deployment (e.g., the GPT-4 model you deployed in the previous step).  
   - **Instructions/Prompt**: enter the system prompt that defines your agent’s role and behavior. For example, “You are an autonomous assistant that helps a developer stay on track by monitoring project activity and suggesting completion strategies.” This prompt guides the agent’s responses.  
   - (Optional) **Knowledge Base**: If your agent needs domain-specific knowledge (documents or data), you can attach a cognitive search index or other knowledge base here. In a basic setup, you might skip this or use default settings.  
   - (Optional) **Tools/Actions**: Azure AI Agent Service allows integration with tools (APIs, code execution, etc.). For UICare, consider enabling the Code Interpreter tool if available, so the agent can execute Python code for analysis. These advanced configurations are optional and can be added later to enhance capabilities【27†L108-L111】.  

   Create the agent and Azure will provision it within your project. You can create multiple agents in one project if needed (for example, a coordinator agent and a worker agent), but for UICare a single agent might suffice initially.

5. **Test the Agent in Azure:** After creation, test your agent using the built-in **Playground**. Azure AI Foundry provides a chat interface to interact with the agent. Click **“Try in playground”** from the agent’s page【27†L115-L123】. In the chat, simulate a scenario (e.g., “I’ve been editing the same code for hours and not making progress, what should I do?”) and verify the agent responds helpfully. This interactive test ensures the agent’s prompt and configuration are correct. Keep in mind the agent is not yet hooked to real project data — it’s operating with the prompt and any provided knowledge. Iteratively refine the instructions or add tools if the responses aren’t as expected.

6. **Connect UICare Extension to the Agent:** With the agent running in Azure, the VS Code extension (or any client application) needs to communicate with it. Azure AI Agent Service exposes a **REST API endpoint** for your project’s agents (it uses the same protocol as Azure OpenAI’s chat API). Retrieve your project’s **connection string or endpoint URL** from the Azure Portal: for example, using Azure CLI you can run `az ml workspace show -n <project_name> -g <resource_group> --query discovery_url` to get the endpoint URL【15†L37-L45】. The endpoint will look like a URL with the region and project ID. In your code (extension), set this as an environment variable (e.g., `AZURE_AI_AGENTS_ENDPOINT`) or configuration setting so the extension knows where to send requests【15†L43-L52】. Also create a **credential** for the agent – Azure Agent Service (being part of Azure AI) likely uses Azure Active Directory for auth, or an API key. The simplest in preview might be Azure OpenAI key or a Managed Identity. For development, you can use an Azure API key or your Azure AD token. **Never hardcode this in code; we’ll cover secret management below**. Once configured, your extension can call the agent’s API to send user inputs and receive the AI responses.

7. **Configuration for Production:** As UICare moves from hackathon prototype to a more production-ready state, consider using a **Standard (single-tenant) setup** for Azure AI Agent Service when available, so you have full control over underlying resources (search index, storage, etc.). The preview’s default (Basic, multi-tenant) is fine for hackathon usage【13†L59-L68】. Monitor the agent’s performance and cost in the Azure Portal (the Azure AI Foundry project page and Azure Monitor logs can show usage). Importantly, set up **resource cleanup or cost limits** if needed – for example, delete the resource group after the event to avoid unexpected charges【27†L131-L139】.

## GitHub Repository Configuration  

Setting up GitHub correctly will streamline collaboration and ensure a robust development workflow for UICare. We’ll address repository naming, branching strategy, CI/CD, secret management, and how to structure the codebases for the VS Code extension and the Azure agent.

### Repository Naming & Structure  
Choose a clear, concise name for your repository (or repositories). For example, if using a single repository, **“UICare”** or **“uicare-agent”** would be appropriate. If you decide to use separate repos for the VS Code extension and the agent service code, use distinct names that reflect each component (e.g., **“UICare-Extension”** and **“UICare-AgentService”**). This clarity helps others (and hackathon judges) identify what each repo contains. In either case, organize the repository content logically: if monorepo, put the VS Code extension source in a subdirectory like `/extension` and any agent-related code (infrastructure scripts, documentation, SDK usage examples) in an `/agent` or `/server` directory. A logical structure makes the codebase easier to navigate and maintain.

**Monorepo vs. Separate Repos:** Both approaches are viable: a monorepo simplifies project management (one place to track issues and PRs, one submission link) and ensures the extension and agent stay in sync, whereas separate repositories enforce a clean separation of concerns (the extension can be open-sourced independently from the agent backend). If you opt for a monorepo, clearly separate the two components in the directory structure and use labels or prefixes in commit messages to indicate which part is affected. If you opt for separate repos, set up each with its own CI/CD and link them in documentation (for instance, the extension README can reference the agent repo for setup, and vice versa). For the hackathon, a monorepo might be simpler to submit, but whichever approach you choose, maintain consistency and clarity across both.

### Branching Strategy  
Adopt a Git branching strategy that supports parallel development and safe integration of changes. The **simplest effective strategy** is to keep a permanent **main** branch for stable code and use **feature branches** for all new development【18†L68-L76】. Developers (or you, even if solo) create feature branches off main (or a dev branch) to work on specific features or fixes, then open a Pull Request to merge back. This ensures the main branch always contains tested, production-ready code【18†L68-L76】. You mentioned using **main/dev branches** – a common approach is to treat **`main`** as the production-ready branch and a **`dev`** branch as the integration branch where ongoing development happens. In this model, feature branches branch off **dev**, merges happen into dev via PRs, and when dev is stable and ready to release (or for a hackathon submission milestone), it’s merged into main. This adds a slight layer of indirection but can be useful if multiple features are in progress.  

No matter which branch model you use, some best practices remain universal: keep branch names consistent and meaningful (e.g. `feature/login-flow` or `fix/memory-leak`), merge changes through Pull Requests (not direct pushes) to enable code review, and **avoid long-lived divergent branches** (integrate changes frequently to prevent massive merge conflicts). Microsoft’s own guidance is to keep the strategy simple and always maintain a high-quality main branch【18†L68-L76】. In a small hackathon team, a lightweight process is fine – even just feature branches off main – as long as you don’t commit unreviewed code to main.

### CI/CD with GitHub Actions  
Set up **Continuous Integration (CI)** early on so that every code change is automatically built and tested. GitHub Actions is an excellent choice for CI in a Node.js/TypeScript project. You can use GitHub’s Node.js CI workflow template as a starting point【36†L336-L344】. In your repository, create a workflow file (e.g., `.github/workflows/ci.yml`) that does the following on each push or pull request to main/dev:  

- **Install Dependencies:** Use the official Node setup action to install the required Node.js version and run `npm install` (or `npm ci` for a clean install).  
- **Build the Code:** If your extension or agent code needs to be transpiled (TypeScript to JavaScript), run the build script (e.g., `npm run build`). This ensures no compile errors.  
- **Run Tests:** Execute the test suite (`npm test`). All unit/integration tests should pass. In a production-hardened project, having tests is crucial to prevent regressions. Even in a hackathon, a basic test or two (for critical logic) can impress judges by showing diligence.  
- **Lint/Format (Optional):** You can include an ESLint or Prettier check to enforce code style, though this is secondary to functional tests.  
- **Artifacts/Deployment (Optional):** As part of CI, you might package the VS Code extension. For example, run `vsce package` to produce a `.vsix` file artifact. This can be attached to workflow results for easy download. If the agent had a deployable component (e.g., an Azure Functions project), CI could also build a Docker image or deploy infrastructure, but in our case the agent logic lives in Azure’s managed service, so deployment might not be applicable beyond ensuring the config scripts (if any) run.  

Using CI ensures that **every PR’s code is verified**. You can require that the CI status check passes before merging (we’ll set that in branch protection)【24†L79-L87】【38†L180-L186】. For Continuous Deployment (CD), you might not do an automated deploy in a hackathon, but you can manually publish the extension to the VS Code Marketplace once it’s ready, or deploy any cloud components. At minimum, have the CI produce ready-to-use outputs (like the extension package or a report) to streamline releasing.

### Managing API Keys and Secrets  
Managing secrets securely is paramount, even in a hackathon project. **Never hardcode API keys, access tokens, or credentials in your repository** – not even in a private repo. This is a critical security best practice: *“Never hardcode secrets in your codebase. Always use GitHub Secrets or secret management tools such as Azure Key Vault.”*【21†L103-L110】. For UICare, the likely secrets include your Azure OpenAI/Agent Service keys or Azure tenant ID/client secrets if using a service principal. Store these in GitHub as **encrypted Secrets** (found under the repo settings > Secrets and variables). In your GitHub Actions workflow, you can reference secrets as environment variables (e.g., `${{ secrets.AZURE_API_KEY }}`) so that CI can access them if needed (for running integration tests or deployment scripts). 

For local development, keep a separate untracked `.env` file or use environment variables to store secrets. That way, you don’t accidentally commit them. If your VS Code extension needs to know the Azure Agent endpoint and a key, consider prompting the user to input their key (or use an OAuth device login flow for Azure) rather than baking it into the code. Since UICare is your project, you might simply read from a config file or environment variable. Just ensure the actual secret value is not in the git history. (If it ever slips, rotate the key immediately.)

GitHub Secrets are encrypted and masked in logs, but also be careful in CI not to accidentally print them (for example, don’t echo the key in the console). With these precautions, your API keys will remain secure【21†L103-L110】. For added safety in a more long-term project, you could integrate Azure Key Vault and have the extension or CI pipeline fetch secrets from the vault, but this might be overkill for a hackathon. 

### Branch Protection Rules  
Once you have multiple collaborators or even just to safeguard the main branch, enable **branch protection** on critical branches (main and dev). Branch protection settings in GitHub allow you to enforce the following rules:  

- **Pull Requests Required:** Disallow direct pushes to the protected branch; all changes must come via merging a pull request. This ensures code is reviewed and CI runs before integration【24†L79-L87】.  
- **Approve Reviews:** Require at least one (or two) approving review on a PR before it can be merged. For a solo project you can skip this, but if working in a team, it’s a good practice (team members reviewing each other’s code).  
- **Status Checks:** Require that the CI workflow passes (e.g., the tests and build are green) before allowing a PR merge. In GitHub branch protection, you’d check “Require status checks to pass before merging” and select your CI checks (like “build” or “test” job)【38†L133-L142】【38†L180-L186】. This guarantees that broken code isn’t merged into main.  
- **Up-to-date Branch:** You can also require that the branch is up-to-date with main before merging (to catch any new conflicts or ensure it includes the latest fixes)【38†L143-L152】. This can be toggled depending on how strict you want to be; it may introduce extra merges in active environments, but it maximizes correctness.  
- **Protect against Force Pushes and Deletions:** Usually, you’d also disable force pushes and prevent deletion of the branch to avoid accidents.  

With these rules in place, you essentially guarantee a clean, stable main branch. As one summary put it, the main branch will only receive changes that went through PR review and passed all tests【38†L178-L186】. For UICare, this means your production-ready code (or final hack submission code) is always in main, and any experimental or ongoing work happens in separate branches without risking the integrity of the main line. Remember to include yourself as an admin exemption if needed (or better, don’t exempt, and just use the same process as everyone). This level of rigor might seem heavy for a hackathon, but demonstrating it will show the judges that your project is maintained with production-level discipline.

### Architecture and Code Separation Considerations  
It’s important to delineate responsibilities between the VS Code extension (client interface) and the Azure agent (backend logic). The VS Code extension should handle the **UI/UX, user input capture, and display of results**. It might also do light preprocessing or local data gathering (for example, collecting recent file edit history if that’s needed by the agent). The Azure AI Agent handles the **heavy AI logic and stateful process automation** – for UICare, this means analyzing the user’s project context (which could be sent over by the extension), detecting the “manic loop” pattern, and orchestrating a solution (perhaps by using its tools or crafting an action plan to show the user). By keeping the extension dumb (just a conduit) and the agent smart, you achieve a modular design. 

【41†embed_image】 For example, the high-level architecture of an AI-driven application might involve a user-facing client (in our case, the VS Code extension) sending requests to an AI agent running in Azure, with various Azure services supporting that agent in the background. In the diagram above (from an Azure sample solution), the **Container App** represents the application code (client) interfacing with the Azure AI Agent Service, and it shows how a Managed Identity can be used for secure resource access to Azure services. In UICare’s context, the VS Code extension would play the role of the client application (instead of a container app), communicating with the Azure AI Agent and possibly triggering other Azure services (like Azure Cognitive Search, if a knowledge base of project data is used). This clear separation ensures that the front-end extension can be developed and tested independently from the agent’s logic. It also makes it easier to swap out or update one side without heavily affecting the other (for instance, you could update the agent’s prompt or tools without needing to change extension code, as long as the interface contract – the API – remains the same). 

From a repository perspective, if you maintain a monorepo, use subfolders to keep this separation (e.g., the extension’s `package.json` and src live under `/extension`, and perhaps an `agent` folder contains any scripts or configs for the Azure side, such as prompt designs, sample API calls, or Bicep templates for provisioning). If using separate repos, each repo will naturally house one piece. In your documentation, explain how the two pieces interact (e.g., “The VS Code extension sends an HTTPS request to the Azure Agent’s endpoint with the user’s project data; the agent responds with an action plan that the extension then presents.”). This will make it easy for someone reading your project to understand the overall system design.

## Hackathon Submission and Production Best Practices  

Beyond just coding, a successful project needs good documentation, licensing, and cost management – especially important for hackathon submissions judged on completeness and professionalism. Below are best practices to **production-harden** UICare and maximize your hackathon score:

- **Comprehensive Documentation:** Treat your GitHub repository as your project’s portfolio. Include a detailed **README** at the root that introduces the project, explains the problem UICare solves, and how your solution works. The README should have sections like **Installation**, **Usage**, and **Architecture**. Explain how to set up the VS Code extension (prerequisites, installation steps), and how to configure it to talk to the Azure agent (e.g., where to put the API endpoint or key). Provide usage examples or screenshots of the extension in action. The documentation should also describe the overall architecture in words and (if possible) with an **architecture diagram**. In fact, hackathon judges will look for a clear description of your solution and may even award points for including an architecture diagram【37†L1-L4】. Ensure your repo also has basic code documentation: inline comments for complex sections, and maybe a separate `docs/` folder if you have additional write-ups or design documents. A well-documented project shows professionalism and helps others (and judges) understand and run your project easily.

- **README Structure and Clarity:** Make your README skimmable and logically organized (use the Markdown headings as you’ve done here). A suggested structure is: **Title and Tagline** (one-liner what UICare is), **Background** (the problem of manic loops in development), **Solution** (how UICare addresses it with AI agents), **Features** (bullet points of what it can do), **Tech Stack** (Azure AI Agent Service, VS Code Extension (Node/TypeScript), etc.), **Setup/Installation** (how to install extension, any Azure setup needed – possibly link to the Azure setup section of your docs), **Usage** (how to trigger the extension and what to expect), **Architecture** (explain agent vs extension, maybe embed the diagram), **Contributing** (if open to contributions), **Team** (mention you as the developer, or team members if any, and contact info), **License**. Use bullet points and short paragraphs so that a judge scanning it in a few minutes can get the gist. The hackathon judging criteria explicitly reward a complete and clear README and repository structure【9†L212-L220】, so this is not just nicety – it’s points on the table.

- **Open Source License:** Add a `LICENSE` file in your repository root. We recommend using an open-source license such as **MIT License** (which is permissive and widely used) unless you have a reason to choose otherwise. An open-source license is important if you want others to use or extend your project; it also signals that you’ve thought about the legal aspect of your code. Many hackathon projects use MIT to simplify things. You can generate one easily (GitHub has a license template picker). Having a license is part of a professional project setup (some companies/judges will check for it, because unlicensed code is technically “All rights reserved” by default). Also, include a brief note in your README that the project is open source under that license. This will encourage the community to adopt or contribute to UICare beyond the hackathon.

- **Security and Privacy Considerations:** Show that you’ve considered the safety of user data and keys. We already covered not committing secrets. Additionally, mention any **Responsible AI** measures if applicable (for example, if your agent might give suggestions that affect real code, how do you ensure it’s safe or reviewed? Perhaps out of scope for now, but a note of awareness can’t hurt). If your project collects any personal data (it likely doesn’t, it’s about code and your own usage), you should mention that it stays local or is used only to feed the AI agent. Microsoft’s hackathon doesn’t want any privacy violations, so you’re likely fine, but being transparent is good.

- **Testing & Quality Assurance:** Even though hackathon timelines are short, try to include at least a few tests or a way to verify the system. If possible, write a couple of unit tests for the extension’s critical logic (for instance, a function that queues user commands for the agent). If writing tests for the agent logic is hard (since it’s mostly in Azure), you might instead document how you manually tested different scenarios. The judging criteria give points for robustness and quality【9†L212-L220】. Also, if you have time, set up a code linter (ESLint) and format the code consistently. This level of polish will make your codebase look production-ready.

- **Azure Cost Management:** Since UICare relies on Azure services, ensure you manage the cloud resources prudently. Microsoft often provides some free credits or free tiers, but if you signed up for a new Azure account you likely have $200 free credit for the first 30 days【27†L62-L64】. Plan your usage to avoid hitting limits: using the **basic agent setup** (managed by Microsoft) means you don’t incur separate costs for search or storage – it’s mostly the cost of model inference. The *gpt-4o-mini* model is chosen to reduce cost; avoid deploying very large models or running the agent endlessly. After the hackathon, **remember to delete or disable resources** to stop billing【27†L131-L139】. You can delete the entire resource group that contains your Azure AI Foundry hub and project to clean up in one go【27†L133-L139】. It’s good to mention in your README’s setup or a separate section how to teardown the environment. This shows that you considered the practical aspect of using cloud resources (a real-world concern). If the hackathon provided Azure sponsorship credits, note how those were used. By demonstrating awareness of cloud cost and cleanup, you make your project more realistic and responsible.

- **Hackathon Submission Extras:** When you’re ready to submit, follow the hackathon instructions closely【28†L31-L38】. Usually, you will need to provide a link to your repo and a short description via their submission form or GitHub Issues template. Make sure every required field is filled. It’s a good idea to prepare a **demo video** (even a 2-3 minute screencast) showing UICare in action – many hackathons appreciate or even require a video. You can upload it to YouTube or elsewhere and link it in your README and submission. A crisp demo will help judges experience your project quickly. Additionally, include some screenshots or GIFs in your README if possible (for example, the extension highlighting a detected issue and the agent’s suggestions). Visuals make the project more engaging. Finally, double-check that your repository is public (if required by the hackathon) and that all documentation is up to date by the submission deadline【28†L29-L37】.

By implementing all the above, the **“Microsoft AI Agents Platform & GitHub Setup”** for UICare will be solid, professional, and production-ready. You’ll have confidence that your Azure agent is configured properly and securely, and your GitHub workflow will reflect software engineering best practices. Not only will this impress hackathon judges (tackling criteria like completeness, innovation, and quality head-on【9†L212-L220】【37†L1-L4】), but it also sets UICare up for future development beyond the hackathon. Good luck with your project, and happy hacking!

## Step 2: Architecture Planning and Diagramming

Use standard Azure architecture icons and notations to communicate clearly【39†L67-L75】【39†L81-L89】. For example, show the flow: *Editor UI → Extension → Agent Service (with RAG and Tools) → External data sources*.

Microsoft notes Azure AI Foundry is a “unified platform for enterprise AI operations” that lets you deploy models and configure agents from VS Code【9†L49-L58】【9†L64-L69】. Plan to use the Foundry extension to manage models, tools, and agents.  
- **Infrastructure Planning:** Determine Azure resources needed (e.g. AI Agent Service instance, Cognitive Search or QnA Maker for RAG, Azure Functions if needed). Consider security architecture (e.g. Azure AD for authentication). Document any Azure-specific configurations in architecture notes.

## Step 3: AI Agent Design and Configuration  
- **Define Agent Roles:** Design the AI agent’s purpose and triggers. For UICare, the agent might monitor developer activity (e.g. repeated commands) and respond with assistance. According to Azure docs, agents are “smart microservices” that can answer questions using RAG, perform actions, or automate workflows【11†L49-L57】. Plan to use RAG by feeding the agent relevant training data or searchable docs so it can answer developer queries.  
- **Use Azure AI Agent Service:** Implement the agent using Azure AI Agent Service, which is a fully managed service for building and scaling AI agents with minimal code【37†L48-L57】【11†L49-L57】. For instance, one can define an agent via the Foundry SDK or YAML that specifies the LLM model, instructions, and available tools【37†L57-L66】【11†L49-L57】.  
- **Configure Tools and Prompt:** Utilize agent tools like Bing Search, Code Interpreter, or File Search to enable the agent to retrieve information or execute code as needed. The Azure AI Foundry VS Code docs show configuring tools via YAML (e.g., a `bing_search` tool) and note that tools such as Bing Grounding or Code Interpreter can be added for capabilities like web search or code execution【24†L107-L115】【24†L119-L123】.  
- **Consider Multi-Agent Orchestration:** Explore using multiple agents in tandem. Microsoft’s example suggests using **Semantic Kernel** to orchestrate agents (e.g. one for planning architecture, another for code generation)【24†L158-L164】【24†L166-L174】. For UICare, one agent might analyze the current developer context while another handles knowledge search. The Foundry docs even recommend starting by creating separate YAML-defined agents (e.g. `DevOpsGenArch` and `DevOpsGenCode`) and orchestrating them【24†L166-L174】.  
- **Responsible AI Features:** Include Responsible AI checks (bias mitigation, filtering). The judging rubric explicitly asks if Responsible AI practices are incorporated【3†L205-L208】. For example, validate that responses are respectful and accurate, add disclaimers in UI, and if feasible log user feedback for continual improvement (human-in-loop).

## Step 4: VS Code Extension Development  
- **Extension Setup:** Initialize a TypeScript-based VS Code extension (e.g. using `yo code`). Structure the extension with a `src/` folder and a `package.json` manifest【41†L168-L177】. In `package.json`, declare activation events and commands under `contributes` so VS Code activates the extension when needed【41†L189-L197】. For instance, define commands like `uicare.getHelp` or `uicare.runAgent`.  
- **User Experience:** Adhere to VS Code UX guidelines. Snyk’s best practices note adding an interactive walkthrough or tutorial if features are complex【18†L456-L462】. Ensure any UI (commands, panels, notifications) feels native. For example, use `vscode.window.showInformationMessage` or a Webview for rich UI. Design with consistent iconography and consider using SVG icons per theme recommendations【18†L458-L462】.  
- **Secure Data Handling:** Store any sensitive data (API keys, tokens) securely. VS Code recommends using the built-in `SecretStorage` API for confidential information【18†L438-L446】. For example, store the Azure API key or token in `SecretStorage` rather than plaintext.  
- **Testing:** Implement thorough testing for the extension. Per VS Code guidance, use the Extension Development Host for integration tests, along with unit tests【18†L420-L428】【18†L428-L432】. For instance, write Mocha/Jest tests that launch a temporary VS Code instance (`vscode-test`) and simulate commands. Snyk emphasizes that extensions must be well-tested before publishing【18†L420-L428】. Include tests for expected outcomes (e.g. the extension correctly calls the agent API).  
- **Bundling:** Follow Microsoft’s advice to bundle the extension for cross-platform compatibility. Use a bundler like esbuild or webpack to produce a single .js file【18†L472-L481】. Snyk notes esbuild is recommended by Microsoft for speed and efficiency【18†L478-L485】.  
- **Documentation:** Throughout code, include clear comments and documentation. The VS Code docs highlight keeping code organized (e.g. commands registered in code matching `package.json`)【41†L136-L144】【41†L189-L197】. Maintain a detailed `README.md` inside the extension folder with usage instructions and development setup, which also supports the solution quality criteria【3†L210-L218】.  

## Step 5: GitHub Monorepo Setup  
- **Monorepo Structure:** Create a single GitHub repository containing both the VS Code extension and the Azure agent service. Place each in its own subdirectory (e.g. `/extension` and `/agent-service`). Using a monorepo simplifies shared configuration and dependency management【6†L96-L99】【22†L75-L83】. In fact, industry practice shows monorepos ease code sharing and consistent tooling across related projects【6†L96-L99】【22†L75-L83】.  
- **Project Layout:** In the root of the repo, include a top-level `README.md` that covers the overall project (with architecture diagram), and sub-folder README files for extension and agent detailing their own build/run steps. Add a `.vscode/launch.json` or relevant configs if helpful. Include `.gitignore` to exclude build artifacts and dependencies (as shown in standard extension layouts【41†L168-L177】).  
- **Dependency Management:** Use a tool for monorepo (optional but recommended). For example, employ Yarn workspaces, npm workspaces, or Microsoft’s NX to manage dependencies of both projects. This ensures a single lockfile and one version for shared modules, aligning with the monorepo benefits of unified versions【6†L96-L99】.  
- **Version Control:** Choose a branching model: A **trunk-based approach** is advised, where developers merge small changes directly into `main` or short-lived branches【42†L3-L10】【7†L7-L15】. Buildkite’s best practices explain that working on a single main branch with frequent merges keeps the codebase consistent and minimizes divergence【42†L3-L10】【42†L13-L16】.  

## Step 6: Branching Strategy and CI/CD Pipeline  
- **Branch Protection:** Enable GitHub branch protection on `main` so that merges require at least one code review and passing CI checks【49†L180-L189】. GitHub’s docs advise using protection rules to enforce requirements (e.g., no direct pushes, mandatory status checks)【49†L180-L189】. This ensures every change is peer-reviewed and validated before integration.  
- **CI/CD Workflows:** Configure GitHub Actions (or Azure Pipelines) for automated building, testing, and deployment. Crucially, scope pipelines to each subproject: set up one workflow for the extension (trigger on changes in `/extension/**`) and another for the agent (trigger on `/agent-service/**`)【29†L520-L528】. The LogRocket guide shows how to use path filters so each subdirectory has its own CI/CD pipeline【29†L520-L528】.  
- **Frequent Builds:** According to Buildkite, every merge to main should trigger automated builds and tests to keep the code “always in a deployable state”【7†L18-L21】. Implement this by running `npm install`/`npm test` for the extension and build/test commands for the agent on each PR/merge. Set the workflows so that the `main` branch only updates if all CI jobs pass, as advised by trunk-based development practices【7†L18-L21】【42†L3-L10】.  
- **Environment Variables:** Store all secrets (Azure credentials, API keys) in GitHub Secrets, not in code. For example, the LogRocket monorepo example stores container registry credentials in Secrets and reads them in the workflow【29†L553-L562】. Similarly, use GitHub Secrets to supply Azure service principals or tokens needed to deploy the agent.  
- **Deployment (Optional):** If time permits, add a CD step to publish the extension to the VS Code Marketplace (using the `vsce` CLI action) and/or deploy the agent to Azure (using Azure CLI or Terraform in CI). This extra polish can demonstrate DevOps completeness.

## Step 7: Testing and Quality Assurance  
- **Unit Tests:** Develop unit tests for both components. For the extension, use a framework like Mocha or Jest with the VS Code test runner. The documentation advises thorough unit testing to catch defects【18†L420-L428】. For the agent service (in Python/C#), use pytest or NUnit to cover core logic (e.g. request handling, RAG logic).  
- **Integration Tests:** Write integration tests to simulate end-to-end scenarios. For example, test that when the extension sends a query to the agent API, the agent returns an expected structured response. Use mocking for external calls (like Azure Cognitive Search) if needed. Run these tests in CI as well.  
- **Automated CI Checks:** Configure the CI pipelines to run linting (e.g. ESLint/TSLint for the extension, pylint/flake8 for Python) and static security checks. This aligns with “security best practices” mentioned in solution quality【3†L210-L218】. Ensuring code quality via automated tools is a real-world DevOps practice.  
- **Continuous Testing:** As Buildkite recommends, ensure every merge triggers the full test suite so that the repo is always in a stable state【7†L18-L21】. Fix any test failures immediately. This way, the master branch remains deployable.  
- **User Acceptance Testing (UAT):** Before submission, manually test the entire workflow: install the extension locally, configure it to use the deployed agent, and verify it behaves as intended. Document any known issues and workarounds.

## Step 8: Documentation and Code Comments  
- **Comprehensive README:** The top-level README should include the project overview, architecture diagram, setup instructions (prerequisites, installation steps), and usage examples. This directly satisfies hackathon criteria for a “complete README”【3†L210-L218】. Explain how to configure Azure resources (e.g. where to set the agent endpoint) so judges can easily run the solution.  
- **Code Comments and Docstrings:** Ensure that both the extension code and the agent code are well-commented. The judges look for code comments in the repository【3†L210-L218】. Use docstrings/functions comments to describe key classes and methods, making the codebase self-explanatory.  
- **Technical Documentation:** Include a brief “Architecture” section in the README describing the main components (using the diagram). Also note any security considerations (e.g., how sensitive data is protected) and responsible AI policies (e.g. data handling, bias checks).  
- **Tutorials/Walkthroughs:** Consider adding a quickstart or tutorial in the README (or as a markdown file) that walks through a typical user scenario. This could be in text or even as step-by-step screenshots. Snyk’s best practices highlight that adding walkthroughs helps new users acclimate【18†L456-L462】.  
- **Contributing and License:** While optional for a hackathon, a short contributing guide or license file can demonstrate professionalism. At minimum, specify any license for open-source code and acknowledge third-party tools.

## Step 9: Demo Video Preparation  
- **Plan the Narrative:** Early in the process, script the demo video. Devpost advises starting demo planning from day one, as judges often see the video first【47†L100-L108】【47†L125-L134】. Identify the key “story”: e.g., present the problem (developer stuck), then show how the extension + agent solves it.  
- **Concise Execution:** Aim for a **3-minute** polished demo. Highlight the innovation and impact quickly, then demo core features: e.g., triggering the agent, receiving helpful advice. Keep it engaging and on-message. Multiple hackathon winners stress a clear script and rehearsal【47†L125-L134】.  
- **Show Real Use-Case:** Use realistic scenarios in the demo. For example, simulate the OCD loop (running the same command) and then show the agent intervening. Demonstrate the extension UI and any output. If possible, capture part of a live coding session.  
- **Quality and Clarity:** Record at high resolution with clear audio. Use captions or callouts to emphasize points. Given judges value UX, show the extension interface (even if minimal) and include labels explaining steps. The goal is to make judges quickly understand what the project does and why it matters.  
- **Review and Polish:** Leave time to iterate on the video. Devpost tips emphasize multiple hours of polishing before submission【47†L133-L142】. Get feedback from peers on clarity and completeness. Ensure the demo also touches on responsible AI aspects if relevant (e.g., mention data privacy or human oversight).

## Step 10: Final Submission and Follow-Through  
- **Package the Entry:** Before the deadline, double-check that all code, docs, and the demo video are in place. The GitHub repo should be public or accessible to judges, with a link in the hackathon submission. Ensure the project submission (e.g. on Devpost or aka.ms/agenthack) includes the repo link, team member list, and any required forms (one per team).  
- **Apply for Bonus Category:** Since UICare includes a VS Code extension, it may qualify for the “Best Use of VS Code Extension” bonus【44†L7-L10】【44†L19-L22】. In the submission, explicitly mention the extension and how it adds value. The hackathon previously noted bonus prizes for extensions that enhance the project【44†L7-L10】【44†L19-L22】.  
- **Compliance Check:** Verify that the project abides by the rules (e.g., uses allowed services, all team members have consented to participate【1†L69-L77】). Confirm no restricted content or plagiarism.  
- **Post-Submission:** Stay available for follow-up from judges. They may ask for clarifications or a short call. Be prepared to showcase the working solution live if requested.  

By following these steps, the UICare project will be well-architected, reliable, and aligned with Microsoft’s hackathon expectations. Each stage leverages proven practices: using Azure AI Foundry and Agent Service for development【9†L49-L58】【37†L48-L57】, applying VS Code extension best practices【18†L420-L428】【18†L438-L446】, enforcing trunk-based Git workflows with CI/CD【42†L3-L10】【29†L520-L528】, and crafting a compelling demo guided by veteran advice【47†L100-L108】【47†L125-L134】. # UICare Technical Architecture

**Overview:** UICare is an AI-powered developer assistant built as a Visual Studio Code extension (frontend) that communicates with a suite of Azure AI Agent backends. The system is designed as a **highly modular, microservice-style architecture**: dozens of fine-grained components handle specific tasks (“do one thing well”), enabling plug‐and‐play substitution and reuse.  The VS Code extension (TypeScript/Node.js) provides the user interface and triggers, while the backend relies on Azure AI Agent Service (via Azure AI Foundry) to run customizable AI agents and tools. This document details each major component, data flows, Azure resources, security, error handling, testing, deployment, and scalability considerations.

## Frontend: VS Code Extension Architecture

The UICare extension runs inside VS Code (Electron) and is written in TypeScript using the official VS Code Extension API.  It is organized into **many small modules**, such as command handlers, UI views, and service classes, following a service-oriented design. The extension’s entry point is `extension.ts` (or `extension.js` after compilation), exporting `activate` and `deactivate` functions【48†L269-L277】. On activation (triggered by user actions or events), the extension initializes services, registers commands, and sets up UI components. For example, the extension manifest (`package.json`) declares commands and activation events (command names, menu contributions, etc.)【24†L191-L199】, and the entry file registers them:

```ts
// extension.ts (simplified)
export function activate(context: vscode.ExtensionContext) {
  // Register a command that asks the AI agent a question
  let askDisposable = vscode.commands.registerCommand('uicare.askAgent', async () => {
    const question = await vscode.window.showInputBox({ prompt: "Ask UICare Agent:" });
    if (question) {
      const response = await AgentService.askQuestion(question);
      vscode.window.showInformationMessage(response);
    }
  });
  context.subscriptions.push(askDisposable);
  // ... register other commands, initialize views/services, etc.
}
```

The extension uses **many individual modules**.  For example:  
- **Command Handlers:** Each command (e.g. “Ask Agent”, “Open Chat”, “Sync Data”) lives in its own TypeScript file.  Each handler imports only the services it needs, promoting single-responsibility.  
- **UI Components:** UI elements (TreeView providers, Webview panels, status bar items, output channels) are modularized. For instance, a `ChatView` module manages the chat window (webview panel) for interacting with agents, while an `AgentsTreeDataProvider` module populates a sidebar list of available agents/projects.  
- **Services:** Backend communication is encapsulated in services. Examples include: an **AzureAuthService** that handles user login with Azure AD (using `@azure/identity`), an **AgentApiClient** that wraps calls to Azure Agent Service (using `@azure/ai-projects` SDK), and a **SecretManager** that wraps `ExtensionContext.secrets` for secure token storage. Each service is instantiated once and shared as needed.  
- **Utility Modules:** Common utilities (e.g. YAML configuration parsing, logging, telemetry, configuration management) are separate modules. For example, a `ConfigManager` reads/writes user settings, and a `Logger` module provides a consistent logging interface to the VS Code Output channel (or to Azure Monitor via Application Insights).  
- **Contribution Points:** The extension can contribute custom views (e.g. an “UICare” sidebar) and use **Context Menus**, **Quick Pick** and **InputBox** dialogs. Each of these is implemented in its own component, making them replaceable or extensible.

**Extension Manifest and Activation:** The `package.json` manifest declares each command, view, and activation event【24†L191-L199】. Activation events (such as `onCommand:uicare.askAgent` or `onView:uicare.chatView`) ensure modules load only when needed. The extension’s entry file (`activate()`) registers each command with `vscode.commands.registerCommand` (as shown above【48†L269-L277】) and pushes disposables to `context.subscriptions` to ensure cleanup. The `deactivate()` function can clean up any long-lived resources (e.g. closing WebSocket connections), though often default disposal via subscriptions suffices【48†L269-L277】.

**Modular Code Example:** The following TypeScript snippet shows how the extension initializes an AI agent and runs a thread using the Azure AI Projects SDK (Node). This function would live in an `AgentApiClient` module:

```ts
import { AIProjectsClient, ToolUtility, MessageStreamEvent } from "@azure/ai-projects";
import { DefaultAzureCredential } from "@azure/identity";

export async function askAgentQuestion(question: string): Promise<string> {
  // Initialize Foundry client from connection string (stored securely in extension settings)
  const connectionString = getFoundryConnectionString(); // e.g. from config or environment
  const client = AIProjectsClient.fromConnectionString(connectionString, new DefaultAzureCredential());
  
  // Example: Create or use an existing agent (here creating a new one for demo)
  const codeTool = ToolUtility.createCodeInterpreterTool([]);
  const agent = await client.agents.createAgent("gpt-4o", {
    name: "uicare-agent",
    instructions: "You are UICare assistant. Answer user queries based on context.",
    tools: [codeTool.definition],
    toolResources: codeTool.resources
  });  //【37†L1169-L1175】

  // Start a conversation thread and post the user question
  const thread = await client.agents.createThread();
  await client.agents.createMessage(thread.id, {
    role: "user",
    content: question
  });  //【37†L1177-L1185】

  // Run the agent (synchronously or streaming)
  const run = await client.agents.createRun(thread.id, agent.id);
  const resultMessages = await client.agents.listMessages(thread.id);
  const assistantReply = resultMessages.data.pop()?.content[0]?.text?.value || "No response";
  
  return assistantReply;
}
```
*Citations:* The above follows Microsoft’s guidance on Azure AI Agents. For example, the agent creation snippet parallels the Python example in Azure docs【31†L649-L656】 and the TypeScript example【37†L1169-L1175】【37†L1177-L1185】.  

**UI Workflows & Data Flow:** A typical user action (e.g. invoking “Ask Agent” from the command palette) flows as follows:

- **User Input:** The user types a question or selects a code snippet.  
- **Extension Handler:** A command handler captures the input and calls the Agent API module (`askAgentQuestion` above).  
- **Backend Call:** The `AgentApiClient` constructs a request to Azure AI Agent Service, including the agent ID and message content.  
- **Agent Processing:** The agent (deployed in Azure Foundry) possibly uses connected tools (see next section) to answer.  
- **Response & Display:** The extension receives the agent’s answer (via SDK call or REST), then displays it in a **Chat View webview** or an information message.

Throughout, the extension remains **stateless and reactive**: no large monolithic component handles everything. Instead, small pieces do single tasks (e.g. one module only deals with Azure auth, another only with displaying chat UI). This design makes it easy to add new features (simply create new modules and commands) without altering unrelated code.

## Backend: Azure AI Agent Service Architecture

The backend is built on **Azure AI Foundry and Azure AI Agent Service (Preview)**. We create an Azure AI Foundry *Hub* and *Project* as the management plane【46†L80-L85】. Within the project, we define multiple **AI Agents**, each acting as a microservice with its own configuration: a chosen LLM (e.g. GPT-4o), custom instructions, and a set of Tools. The agent service is fully managed by Azure, abstracting underlying compute and storage【2†L48-L57】.

**Major Components:**  
- **Foundry Hub & Project:** The hub is the top-level resource (an AML workspace) that provides shared networking, security, and compute【46†L80-L85】. The hub has an associated Storage account and ACR by default. Inside the hub, one or more *Projects* are created (each is also an ML workspace) to isolate data and resources per team or app. Each Project inherits the hub’s managed network. The hub and project use **Managed Identities** for secure access to Azure resources.  
- **Agents:** Each agent is an entity with a model (Azure OpenAI endpoint) and Tool list. Agents run on **Managed Online Endpoints** behind the scenes.  Agents communicate via *Threads* and *Messages*【31†L659-L667】.  Developers can create, update, and delete agents programmatically or via YAML. Each agent’s role is focused (e.g. “UICareCodeAgent”, “UICareSearchAgent”, “UICareSchedulerAgent”).  
- **Tools:** Tools extend agent capabilities by allowing them to call external services. UICare integrates built-in tools (Code Interpreter, File Search, OpenAPI/function calling) and custom ones. For example:  
  - **Code Interpreter Tool:** Allows agents to execute code (Python/TypeScript) in a sandbox.  
  - **Azure Search Tool:** Using Cognitive Search for knowledge retrieval (RAG).  
  - **HTTP/OpenAPI Tool:** Agents can call custom REST APIs (e.g. a service for medical data or issue tracking).  
  - **Calendar or DB Tools:** Agent can query Azure SQL or Graph APIs via custom connectors.  
  - Each tool is implemented as its own microservice or function. For instance, a `BillingInfoTool` might be an Azure Function that fetches data from an external system. The agent simply calls it via defined OpenAPI schema.  

**Interactions and Data Flows:** The core flow is: **User (VS Code) → Agent Service → (LLM + Tools) → Response**.  For example, when the user asks a question, the extension’s AgentApiClient uses the Azure AI Foundry SDK to `createMessage` in a thread, then `createRun` to execute the agent【31†L649-L657】【31†L658-L666】. Behind the scenes, the agent uses the LLM to generate responses and may invoke tools: e.g. it might query an Azure Cognitive Search index for relevant documents or run code via Code Interpreter. Responses (text, images, or file links) are returned as Message objects to the extension.

【22†embed_image】 *Figure: Reference architecture for UICare backend. The VS Code extension communicates with Azure AI Foundry (via private endpoints), which routes queries to a managed Online Endpoint (AI Agent). The agent can call Azure OpenAI for LLM work, Azure Cognitive Search for data lookup, and other Azure services (Functions, SQL, etc.). Data flows back to the extension.*  
*Diagram notes:* The figure shows an architecture where the extension (left) calls an Azure App Service (representing any custom API) and an Azure AI Online Endpoint (for the agent). The agent endpoint (right) is in a secured VNet with managed identity, connected to Azure Key Vault, Storage, ACR, LLM endpoints (e.g. Azure OpenAI), and Azure AI Search (Cognitive Search).  

**Azure Resources:** We leverage the following key Azure resources (many of which are created by Foundry when setting up the hub/project【46†L141-L149】):  
- **Azure AI Foundry (Hub/Project):** Organizes resources. Hub uses AML resource provider.  
- **Azure OpenAI Service:** Provides GPT models (via Foundry connections). Foundry “connects to Azure OpenAI” as a linked service【46†L80-L85】.  
- **Azure Cognitive Search (“AI Search”):** Used for Retrieval-Augmented Generation. We create an Azure Cognitive Search service and index domain data (e.g. documentation, knowledge base). This is connected to the Foundry project as an external resource. Agents use it via built-in search tools or REST calls.  (Azure doc: “AI Search provides search capabilities for your projects”【46†L141-L149】.)  
- **Azure Storage Account:** Used by Foundry to store artifacts (flows, datasets)【46†L141-L149】. Also used by us to store any uploaded user files or logs.  
- **Azure Key Vault:** Manages secrets (database credentials, API keys)【46†L141-L149】.  Foundry itself secures connection strings here. Our custom code also retrieves secrets from Key Vault at runtime, using managed identities, so no keys are hard-coded.  
- **Azure Container Registry (ACR):** Stores any custom container images for prompt flows or tools【46†L148-L152】. For example, if we deploy a custom Docker container for a translation tool, it lives in ACR.  
- **Azure Functions / App Service:** Host custom API endpoints (if needed). For example, a `HealthDataFunction` might pull patient info (placeholder example) when an agent calls it. These are secured via Managed Identity and VNet.  
- **Azure Application Insights & Log Analytics:** Capture telemetry from deployed prompt flows or functions (optionally enabled via Foundry)【13†L154-L158】. We log agent interactions, performance metrics, and errors.  
- **Azure Active Directory (Azure AD):** Used for authentication and RBAC. Users are granted roles (AI Services Developer, etc.) to manage the Foundry resources. The extension uses Azure AD (via `DefaultAzureCredential`) to authenticate to Foundry【31†L639-L647】【37†L1161-L1169】.  

**Agent Code Example:** The following Python snippet (using the Azure AI Foundry SDK) illustrates creating an agent, posting a message, and running it【31†L649-L656】【31†L659-L667】:

```python
from azure.ai.projects import AIProjectClient
from azure.ai.projects.tools import CodeInterpreterTool
from azure.identity import DefaultAzureCredential

client = AIProjectClient.from_connection_string(os.environ["PROJECT_CONNECTION_STRING"], credential=DefaultAzureCredential())
with client:
    code_tool = CodeInterpreterTool()
    agent = client.agents.create_agent(
        model="gpt-4o-mini",
        name="uicare-agent-py",
        instructions="You are UICare assistant. Provide helpful answers.",
        tools=code_tool.definitions,
        tool_resources=code_tool.resources
    )  #【31†L649-L656】
    thread = client.agents.create_thread()
    message = client.agents.create_message(thread_id=thread.id, role="user",
        content="Generate a summary of the code in main.py")
    run = client.agents.create_and_process_run(thread_id=thread.id, agent_id=agent.id)
    print(f"Run status: {run.status}, last error: {run.last_error}")
```

*Citations:* This aligns with Microsoft’s quickstart code for Azure AI Agent Service【31†L649-L657】. The snippet shows creating an agent with the CodeInterpreter tool and sending a user message.  In practice, our agents would be pre-created (often via YAML or Foundry portal) and reused rather than recreated on every call; here it’s illustrative.

**Error Handling & Monitoring:** In both extension and agent, errors are handled explicitly. For example, after a run completes we check `run.status`; if it is `"failed"`, we log `run.last_error` and take action (e.g. retry or notify user)【31†L678-L682】. We also watch for common issues like rate limits (the code comment suggests requesting higher quota if “Rate limit is exceeded”). Agents can raise exceptions if tools fail; we catch them and return a safe fallback message. All errors are logged. In production, we enable **Application Insights** tracing on deployed flows【13†L154-L158】 to capture exceptions and performance. The architecture isolates failures: if one agent or tool fails, it doesn’t crash others. Transient faults use retry policies, and tools/services are behind circuit-breakers.

## Authentication & Security

UICare enforces strong security. **Authentication:** The user must sign into Azure (via VS Code sign-in) to use the extension. We use the Azure Identity SDK (`DefaultAzureCredential`)【31†L639-L647】【37†L1161-L1164】, which may prompt the user to log in (device code or browser). The extension does _not_ hardcode any keys; access tokens are cached in VS Code’s secure SecretStorage (`ExtensionContext.secrets`). The Agent Service (Foundry) uses Azure AD RBAC roles (e.g. “AI Services Developer”) to control who can create/edit agents. 

**Secret Management:** All secrets (API keys, connection strings) live in Azure Key Vault【46†L141-L149】. The extension retrieves needed secrets at runtime using managed identity or AAD tokens – for example, it uses a service principal or user token to fetch the Foundry project connection string from Key Vault. Tools running in Azure (Functions, containers) use their system-assigned managed identities to access Key Vault, so credentials are never in code or config. 

**Network Security:** The Foundry hub and project are deployed into a **managed virtual network** with private endpoints (for Storage, Key Vault, ACR)【4†L59-L62】【46†L80-L85】. This means traffic between the extension (via Azure Portal or CLI) and the Azure resources is secured. Any custom App Service or Function used by agents can be placed behind an **App Gateway with WAF** or restricted with service endpoints. All communication uses HTTPS/TLS.

**Application Security:** The extension enforces workspace trust (disables features if the workspace is untrusted), and all API calls to Azure use official SDKs or HTTPS clients. We validate inputs (e.g. sanitize user messages or filenames before using them as prompts). For sensitive operations, we confirm user intent (e.g. before deleting an agent). No PII is logged outside secure stores. In the agent context, we employ Content Filters (if provided by the model) to avoid unsafe outputs.

## Error Handling and Resilience Patterns

UICare applies robust resilience patterns:

- **Retries and Backoff:** For transient Azure errors (timeouts, rate limits), calls are retried with exponential backoff. The SDKs (Azure SDKs, Foundry SDK) have built-in retry logic for idempotent operations.  
- **Time-outs:** Each external call (Azure API, LLM call, tool invocation) has a reasonable timeout (e.g. 30s for LLM, configurable). If exceeded, the operation is aborted and an error returned to the user.  
- **Circuit Breakers:** If a downstream service (e.g. custom Function) is failing repeatedly, we trip a circuit breaker to prevent overload, then periodically test for recovery.  
- **Graceful Degradation:** If a certain tool fails, the agent can continue with reduced capability. For example, if Cognitive Search is down, the agent might answer without RAG, or vice versa.  
- **Logging and Alerts:** We log all exceptions and abnormal conditions to Azure Monitor/AppInsights. Alerts can be set on error rates or latency (via Log Analytics) to notify devs immediately.  
- **Isolation:** Each agent run is isolated. A crash in one thread or agent does not stop the service. Agents and tools run on managed endpoints/containers; unhealthy ones can be auto-restarted by Azure.  
- **Validation:** We validate all user inputs to tools (e.g. file paths, URLs) to avoid injection attacks. In the UI, invalid inputs produce friendly error messages without crashing the extension.

## Testing Strategy

Given the criticality of accuracy and reliability, we implement a comprehensive testing regimen:

- **Unit Testing:**  
  - *Extension:* We use Mocha/Jest with the [vscode-test](https://code.visualstudio.com/api/working-with-extensions/testing-extension) framework to unit-test each module. Command handlers, services, and utility functions are tested in isolation. VS Code API calls are mocked. For example, we test that the “Ask Agent” command constructs the correct API request.  
  - *Backend:* Custom tool code (Python/TS for Azure Functions) is unit-tested with PyTest or Jest. Agent logic (if any code-based steps) is validated against known inputs.  
- **Integration Testing:**  
  - *Extension-Backend:* We deploy a test Foundry project in a non-production Azure subscription. Automated tests (using Mocha or a custom script) send requests through the extension to ensure end-to-end functionality. We mock less critical dependencies to focus on core flows.  
  - *Agents & Tools:* We use Azure DevOps pipeline steps to run integration tests against the live agent endpoints. For example, we create test threads, send messages, and verify the responses contain expected patterns. Agents are tested with canned prompts (unit-test style) to ensure they call the correct tools and handle responses.  
- **Load and Performance Testing:**  
  - We simulate concurrent usage (e.g. 100 users) by scripting parallel agent calls (using tools like Artillery or Locust). We measure latency of agent responses and underlying Azure resource usage. Cognitive Search is tested with large indexes to ensure query performance. The architecture uses Azure’s auto-scaling (foundry-managed), but we validate that under high load the throughput and cost remain acceptable.  
- **Security Testing:**  
  - Use static analysis (npm audit, bandit, etc.) to scan for vulnerabilities in code.  
  - Penetration tests on custom APIs and permission reviews for Azure resources.  
- **Continuous Testing in CI:** All tests run in GitHub Actions on every PR. The extension is linted (ESLint/TSLint) and type-checked. Any failures block merges.

## Deployment Strategy (GitHub CI/CD)

We employ GitHub Actions for CI/CD for both the extension and the backend.

- **VS Code Extension Pipeline:** On each commit to `main` (and for PRs), we run:
  1. **Lint & Build:** `npm install`, compile TypeScript, run ESLint.  
  2. **Unit Tests:** Run Mocha/Jest tests.  
  3. **Pack and Publish:** If on `main`, use `vsce` (the VS Code Extension CLI) to package and publish the extension to the Marketplace under our publisher ID. Release notes and version are auto-generated from commits. We use a signing key from GitHub Secrets.  
  4. **Security Checks:** Optionally run a security scan (e.g. Synk).  
  5. **Gateway:** Output includes a link to the Marketplace listing for download (for internal distribution or validation).  

- **Backend Infrastructure Pipeline:** Infrastructure and agent definitions are managed as code (e.g. Bicep/Terraform templates and YAML for agents).
  1. **Template Deployment:** Using GitHub Actions with `Azure/login` and `Azure/cli` actions, we deploy the Foundry hub and project (if not existing) and dependent resources via ARM/Bicep. For new environments, we parameterize resource names.  
  2. **Agent Deployment:** We deploy agents via Azure CLI or REST (for standard setup) or upload YAML definitions via the Foundry CLI. The pipeline also pushes any updated promptflow code or custom tool images to ACR.  
  3. **Integration Tests:** After deployment, pipeline runs smoke tests (using Azure CLI or scripts) to ensure the Foundry project is reachable and agents respond.  
  4. **Monitoring Setup:** Ensure Application Insights and alerts are configured (via IaC or post-deployment script).  
  5. **Secrets Management:** Sensitive variables (e.g. Key Vault names, service principal credentials) are stored in GitHub Secrets and referenced via the pipeline.  

- **Versioning and Environments:** We maintain `dev`, `staging`, and `prod` branches/environments. Merges to `staging` trigger deployment to a staging Azure subscription, while `prod` triggers production deploy. 
- **Reviews and Quality Gates:** PRs require code reviews, and CI must pass all tests. We use Terraform plan/apply reviews for infra changes. 
- **Documentation:** The architecture doc and usage guides are updated and published automatically with the code (e.g. as Markdown files in the repo, rendered in a wiki).

## Scalability and Extensibility

**Scalability:**  
- *Backend:* Azure AI Agent Service is designed to autoscale. Foundry-managed compute endpoints can scale out (multiple replicas) under load, especially for models like GPT-4o. Cognitive Search can scale with replicas/shards. The architecture uses separate services (Stateless APIs, Functions) that scale independently. By adhering to cloud-native stateless design, UICare can handle growing user load seamlessly.  
- *Data:* Knowledge bases in Cognitive Search can be partitioned; we use vector or hybrid search which scales better. We plan for sharding data if needed. LLM calls are parallelized per thread.  
- *Extension:* The client side scales by virtue of being distributed on each user’s machine. Any heavy processing is offloaded to Azure.

**Extensibility:**  
- *Modularity:* By having ~100+ small components (e.g. dozens of commands, UI widgets, service classes), new features can be added easily. For example, adding a new “DatabaseQuery” tool simply means writing a new Azure Function (or Logic App) and adding its OpenAPI spec to the agent’s YAML. The extension can add a new command linked to this tool without impacting other code.  
- *Plugins and Flows:* The agents themselves are versioned/configured in Foundry. We can update agents by uploading new YAML or prompt flows. The UI allows developers to manage agents on the fly (leveraging VS Code commands to open Foundry portal or directly edit YAML).  
- *Support for New Models:* The architecture supports swapping or adding LLMs. For instance, we can deploy a custom fine-tuned model endpoint and simply point the agent’s `model` to it.  
- *Cross-Platform:* While the extension targets VS Code, the core Agent Service is decoupled and could serve other clients (a web UI or mobile app) with minimal changes.  
- *Future AI Capabilities:* The system is built to incorporate future Azure AI services. For instance, if a new Azure AI skill appears (e.g. advanced vision or speech-to-text), it can be integrated as a new tool or service. The Foundry “Tool” abstraction makes plugging in new capabilities straightforward.  

Each module or microservice is documented and discoverable. We use consistent interfaces (e.g. JSON for messages, REST APIs for tools) so new teams can build their own components. For example, a team could contribute a “Scheduling Agent” by writing a new module that follows our patterns (registers a command, defines a tool, etc.) without altering UICare’s core code.
