# UICare System – Technical Architecture Blueprint

## Overview  
This architecture uses a microservices pattern: each component is an independent service focused on one capability【14†L39-L44】. The VS Code extension (TypeScript) serves as the frontend, invoking backend AI agents hosted in Azure. The backend orchestrates Azure AI Agent Service (“Foundry”) to run agents that analyze the user’s code and context. Agents use Azure OpenAI models and tools like Azure AI Search for data retrieval【3†L119-L124】. All services communicate via secure HTTP/API calls and are deployed in Azure (App Service, Foundry, etc.). The system is fully containerized/PaaS, stateless where possible, and instrumented with logging/metrics for monitoring and alerting.

## Frontend – VS Code Extension (TypeScript)  
- **Platform & Language**: Build a VS Code extension in TypeScript using the official VS Code Extension API. Use a modern toolchain (e.g. Node.js, Webpack or esbuild) and strong linting/types (TSConfig).  
- **Functionality**: The extension implements explicit commands or UI elements to invoke UICare features. On user action, it gathers context (open project files, unsaved edits, project metadata) and sends it to the backend via REST/HTTP. All communication is JSON over HTTPS. The extension does **not** contain secrets or direct AI logic; it delegates processing to the backend.  
- **Authentication**: The extension will use Azure AD for user identity. It must obtain an AAD access token (using MSAL) and include it in API calls to the backend. No hardcoded credentials are used. The extension’s `package.json` manifest declares its AAD application ID and permissions.  
- **Error Handling & UX**: Handle API errors gracefully. Show clear messages or retry options in the UI. The extension validates input before sending (e.g. non-empty prompt) and performs local sanity checks.  
- **Testing**: Implement unit tests (e.g. Mocha, Jest) for all extension logic. Mock VS Code APIs and HTTP calls. Add integration tests that simulate user commands and stub backend responses. Integrate these tests into the CI pipeline.  

## Backend – Azure AI Agent Service via Foundry  
- **Core Service**: Host a backend service in Azure App Service (Linux), written in a single language (e.g. Node.js or Python). This service exposes REST endpoints (e.g. `/api/ai-session`, `/api/health`) consumed by the VS Code extension. The service is stateless (scale-out with containers) and uses Azure AD authentication (validate the extension’s tokens).  
- **AI Agents**: Use Azure AI Agent Service (Foundry) to create and run AI agents. Define one or more agent configurations (via the Foundry SDK) that load a GPT model (e.g. GPT-4o) with instructions and tools. Agents include:  
  - **Project Analyzer Agent** – scans project files (via Azure AI Search tool) to understand objectives.  
  - **Code Generator Agent** – uses Azure OpenAI (code-enabled models) to produce code snippets or summaries.  
  - **Workflow Coordinator Agent** – orchestrates multi-agent pipelines (e.g. triggers Project Analyzer then Code Generator).  
- **Tools & Integrations**: Leverage built-in Foundry tools. For example, integrate **Azure AI Search** as an agent tool for retrieving indexed project content【3†L119-L124】. Use the OpenAI “code interpreter” tool for executing or linting code if needed. If custom actions are required, use Azure Functions or protected APIs as tools for the agent.  
- **Data Flow**: The backend receives a request (with project context) and initiates an agent thread via Foundry SDK. It passes the context to the Project Analyzer Agent, which may query the Azure Search index. Agents communicate via Foundry’s threading; the backend polls the thread until completion. Final results (e.g. summaries or generated code) are returned to the extension.  
- **Storage & State**: Store session history, logs, and intermediate data in Azure Blob Storage or Cosmos DB. Also use this storage to persist agent activities. For temporary agent state (e.g. thread IDs), rely on Foundry’s managed state (threads keep conversation).  
- **API Security**: All backend endpoints require a valid Azure AD token. Validate tokens with Microsoft libraries. Enforce strict input/output schemas. No anonymous access. 

## Component Modularization  
【15†embed_image】 The system follows a microservices architecture: a client (VS Code) calls an API gateway (the backend service), which routes to multiple small services【14†L39-L44】. Each service is a distinct module, deployed independently. Key modules include:
- **Auth Module** – handles Azure AD token validation for all requests.  
- **Agent Manager** – configures and invokes Foundry agents (using the Foundry SDK). This is the core orchestrator.  
- **Search Service** – a module that communicates with Azure Cognitive Search for indexing and querying project data.  
- **OpenAI Service** – wraps calls to Azure OpenAI (Azure OpenAI Service), retrieving models via Key Vault-stored keys.  
- **Data Ingestion** – module to preprocess and upload project files into the Search index.  
- **Storage Service** – handles interactions with Azure Blob or Cosmos DB for saving session data and logs.  
- **Utilities & Common** – shared code (validation, logging, retry logic, models).  
Each module is a self-contained library or microservice “doing one thing well,” facilitating up to 100–150 such modules. They communicate via well-defined interfaces. Modules are kept small to ensure single responsibility and easy testing【14†L39-L44】.  

## Azure Infrastructure & Resources  
All Azure resources are provisioned via Infrastructure-as-Code (Azure Bicep or ARM). Organize resources per environment (dev/test/prod) in separate resource groups. Key resources:  
- **Azure AI Foundry (Machine Learning) Hub & Project** – Create an Azure ML Workspace acting as the Foundry Hub, with a dedicated Azure Storage and Key Vault for Foundry data【30†L75-L83】【30†L141-L149】. Under the hub, create a Foundry Project for the AI agent deployments. Configure network isolation (VNet) if needed.  
- **Azure OpenAI Service** – Provision an Azure OpenAI instance with required model deployments (e.g. GPT-4o). Store its endpoint/keys in Key Vault.  
- **Azure Cognitive Search** – Provision an AI-capable Search service. Use it to index code and documentation for RAG queries【36†L74-L77】. Connect this Search service to the Foundry hub (as a Foundry connection) and also to the backend.  
- **Azure App Service** – Create an App Service plan and a Linux web app for the backend API. Enable autoscale. Associate a Managed Identity. (Optionally use Azure Container Apps or Kubernetes if needed for scalability.)  
- **Azure Storage Account** – General-purpose storage for logs, file blobs, etc. Restrict access via network rules and assign roles to services using MI.  
- **Azure Key Vault** – One Key Vault per environment to store all secrets (OpenAI keys, Search admin keys, etc). Do not use other secret stores or static configs. Configure firewall, purge protection, and turn on logging【38†L95-L98】.  
- **Azure AD (Entra)** – Use Azure AD for all identity. Create a user/app registration for the VS Code extension (for user login) and rely on Managed Identities for service calls. No app stores embedded credentials.  
- **Azure Monitor / Application Insights** – Enable Application Insights on App Service for telemetry. Collect logs from all components. Define alerts on exceptions, latency, or failed agent runs.  

## Authentication & Secret Management  
**Managed Identities**: Assign a managed identity to every Azure service (App Service, Functions, etc). Use **user-assigned managed identities** for consistency across multiple resources【19†L163-L172】. All service-to-service calls (to Key Vault, Search, Storage, AI services) must use Azure AD tokens from these identities. Grant the *minimum* RBAC roles needed (principle of least privilege)【19†L188-L197】. Do not use connection strings or keys in code.  
**Key Vault Only**: Store *all* sensitive configuration and secrets in Azure Key Vault. This includes AI API keys, database connection strings, etc. Do not store any secrets in code, config files, or GitHub. Use Azure SDKs (e.g. Azure.Identity) to fetch secrets at runtime【5†L78-L87】. Restrict Key Vault access with RBAC and network rules; do not use legacy access policies【38†L95-L98】.  
**Authentication Flow**: The VS Code extension obtains an Azure AD token for the backend (using the user’s identity). The backend validates this token and authorizes actions. All Azure APIs (OpenAI, Search, Storage) are accessed via Azure AD – no other credential mechanism is used. Ensure tokens and secrets are never logged.  

## Error Handling & Resilience  
Design for failure. Implement standard patterns:  
- **Retries**: Wrap all external calls (Azure APIs, HTTP requests) in retry policies with exponential backoff【24†L98-L104】. Retry on transient faults (HTTP 5xx, timeouts). After a configurable max attempts, propagate an error. Log only final failures as errors; log interim failures at warning or info level【24†L98-L104】.  
- **Circuit Breakers**: Use a circuit-breaker pattern for critical dependencies (e.g. OpenAI). After successive failures (e.g. 5), open the circuit for a short period (e.g. 30s) so calls fail fast【26†L141-L149】. Provide fallback handling or user notification if services are unavailable.  
- **Validation**: Perform strict input validation on all incoming data (API payloads, agent results). Sanitize content before passing to AI models. Validate outputs before returning to user.  
- **Timeouts and Bulkheads**: Apply timeouts to all external calls. Consider isolating heavy operations (e.g. long-running agent workflows) behind separate services or job queues.  
- **Logging and Monitoring**: Instrument all code paths with structured logs and metrics. Use Azure Application Insights or Log Analytics to capture errors and performance. Configure alerts on repeated failures or latencies.  
- **Idempotency and State**: Design operations to be idempotent where possible. If an agent run fails, allow retrying without side effects. Store state consistently so partial failures can recover.  

## Testing Strategy  
Achieve full test coverage at multiple levels:  
- **Unit Tests**: Every module has unit tests covering all logic branches. Use frameworks like Jest (for Node) or PyTest (for Python). Mock external calls (Azure services, Foundry SDK) to simulate success and error conditions. Aim for ≥90% code coverage.  
- **Integration Tests**: Test the end-to-end flow in a staging environment. This includes the VS Code extension invoking the backend and triggering a full agent run. Use real or test Azure services (with test data). Verify correct behavior under realistic conditions.  
- **Load Testing**: Perform load tests on the backend and AI services. Use tools (e.g. Azure Load Testing, JMeter, or k6) to simulate concurrent users triggering multiple agents. Validate the system scales and identify bottlenecks.  
- **Security Tests**: Include static code analysis (linting, dependency scanning), container image scanning, and penetration tests. Use Azure Security Center recommendations. Check for OWASP Top 10 issues in the API and extension.  
- **Automated in CI**: Integrate tests into GitHub Actions. CI pipelines must build the code and run all unit/integration tests on every pull request. Fail the pipeline on any test or build error.  

## GitHub Monorepo Structure  
Use a single GitHub repository with a clear directory layout. Example structure:  
```
/docs                   ← Architecture diagrams, README, deployment guides  
/src                   
  /extension            ← VS Code extension code (TypeScript)  
    - package.json, src/ 
  /backend              ← Backend service (Node or Python)  
    - src/, package.json (or requirements.txt)  
  /agents               ← Foundry agent configurations or scripts  
  /common               ← Shared utilities (logging, auth, models)  
/tests                 
  /unit                 ← Unit tests for each component  
  /integration          ← Integration tests (simulate full flows)  
  /load                 ← Load-test scripts/config  
README.md              ← Project overview, setup instructions  
```
Each folder is an isolated module/package (using npm workspaces or project references). Use CI tools (ESLint, Prettier) to enforce coding standards across the repo. All code and docs are version-controlled together, ensuring atomic changes.  

## CI/CD Pipeline (GitHub Actions)  
Implement fully automated pipelines using GitHub Actions:  
1. **Continuous Integration (CI)** on every push/PR:  
   - Install dependencies and compile code.  
   - Run linters, static analysis, unit tests, and security scans.  
   - Package the VS Code extension.  
   - Publish code coverage reports.  
2. **Continuous Deployment (CD)** on merge to main or release tags:  
   - Use Azure’s recommended GitHub Action with OpenID Connect to authenticate (no secrets)【11†L70-L77】. Configure a *user-assigned managed identity* for the workflow.  
   - Deploy backend code to the Azure App Service (e.g. using `azure/webapps-deploy` action).  
   - Update Azure AI Foundry projects if needed (can use Azure CLI or SDK scripts).  
   - Deploy static assets or extension package if distributing to Marketplace.  
   - Tag the release and update versioning.  
   - Upon failure at any stage, halt and notify via PR status or email.  
Use GitHub Environments to require approval before deploying to production. Secure pipeline secrets in GitHub (only for non-Azure credentials, if any, though mostly MI is used) and rotate them per policy.  

## Documentation & Diagrams  
Maintain comprehensive documentation:  
- **README**: High-level project overview, architecture summary, setup instructions, and deployment steps. Include environment variable and secret setup (pointing to Key Vault).  
- **Design Docs**: Use `/docs` for detailed architecture design, resource diagrams, and component descriptions. Include a sequence or flow diagram of the agent workflow (created via Mermaid or Visio).  
- **Code Comments**: All public functions/types are documented (TSdoc/JSDoc or XML comments). Non-obvious logic has inline comments. Keep comments in sync with code behavior.  
- **Diagrams**: Provide architecture diagrams illustrating the solution (C4 or Azure icons). For example, a diagram showing the VS Code extension -> API Gateway -> Agents -> Azure services. Embed diagrams in the docs (PNG or SVG) with captions.  
- **Versioning**: Document API endpoints and version in README. Update docs as code changes.  

## Scalability & Extensibility  
- **Horizontal Scaling**: All services are stateless and can be scaled out. Configure auto-scale rules on the App Service. If using containers, leverage Azure Container Instances or AKS with autoscaling. For Foundry, scale up the hub’s compute if agents need more resources.  
- **Modularity**: The microservices design allows adding new services/agents without impacting others. New agent types or tools can be implemented as new modules in `/agents` and deployed via CI.  
- **Azure Services**: Rely on Azure PaaS for managed scaling. For example, Azure Cognitive Search can be scaled with replicas or partitions. Use partitioned storage for large data.  
- **Feature Flags**: Implement feature flags (in config/Key Vault) to enable/disable new capabilities at runtime, easing gradual rollout.  
- **Monitoring for Growth**: Continuously monitor load and performance (through App Insights). Provision additional resources (Compute tiers, storage throughput) as usage grows. Document how to add regions or tenants if multi-region support is needed.  

Each decision above chooses a single, production-grade approach without ambiguity. This blueprint is directly actionable: engineers can follow each section step-by-step to build the UICare system in a secure, scalable, and well-documented manner.

# Recommended Next Step: Complete the Agent Design Specification

- **Design-first approach:** Industry best practice is to finalize a software design specification (SDD) before writing code【32†L80-L88】. This comprehensive “blueprint” aligns all stakeholders on the agent’s architecture and requirements. Defining the agent’s components and behavior upfront ensures clarity for the team and reduces miscommunications and design flaws【32†L98-L101】. It also serves as a single source of truth that prevents scope creep and identifies risks early【32†L106-L112】.  

- **Azure AI Agent focus:** Azure’s managed AI Agent Service lets developers define an agent with just a few lines of code by specifying the model, instructions, and tools【42†L105-L110】【43†L127-L130】. Since the platform handles compute, security, and integration, the team’s effort should be on detailing the agent’s logic and data needs (the design) rather than worrying about infrastructure. In practice, this means designing the agent’s goals and workflows first, then relying on Azure to automate deployment.

- **DevOps readiness:** Production-grade delivery requires early establishment of CI/CD pipelines and repository governance【46†L189-L199】. Automated pipelines, branch protection, and pull-request policies are essential to enforce code quality (pipeline runs *mandatory* to keep quality high)【46†L197-L199】. However, these DevOps configurations depend on a clear project structure. It is therefore safer to implement monorepo scaffolding and pipeline automation *after* the design is locked in, ensuring that the repository layout and build processes align with the planned architecture.

- **Hackathon vs. production:** Hackathon-winning teams often prioritize speed with a code-first, rapid-prototyping mindset【48†L84-L88】. While this can accelerate early coding, it can lead to misalignment later. In contrast, a design-first (spec-first) approach has been shown to produce more consistent and maintainable results【48†L102-L105】. By agreeing on the API/agent definition early, all team members (developers, testers, writers) can work in parallel and avoid rework. For UICare’s production-quality goals, completing the agent design spec upfront will save time and reduce risk in the long run.

**Recommendation:** **Complete the Agent Design Specification first.** This design-first approach (backed by Azure AI Foundry guidelines and industry case studies) provides a clear roadmap and reduces project risk【32†L80-L88】【46†L189-L199】. Once the agent’s architecture and requirements are finalized, scaffolding the GitHub monorepo and CI/CD pipeline can proceed rapidly and with greater confidence in the overall solution. 

**Sources:** Best practices for software design and DevOps quality assurance【32†L80-L88】【32†L98-L101】【46†L189-L199】, Microsoft Azure AI Agent documentation【42†L105-L110】【43†L127-L130】, and industry analyses of code-first vs design-first approaches【48†L84-L88】【48†L102-L105】.

# UICare Agent Design Specification

## Agent Personas  
The UICare system comprises three specialized AI agents, each with a clear role in a coordinated workflow【39†L132-L139】.  Each agent has a deterministic persona and goals, ensuring that tasks are assigned and executed without confusion. All agents use supportive, professional language, preserve user control, and avoid blame or negative wording【15†L69-L72】【55†L205-L209】.

- **Monitor Agent** – *Role:* Continuously observe the user’s activity and project state to detect signs of cognitive distress (e.g. obsessive loops, repetitive actions, stalled progress).  
  *Goals:* Identify when the user is “stuck” or exhibiting frustration so that intervention can occur before work is lost.  *Capabilities:* Analyzes user interaction logs and current workspace context (using Code Interpreter for pattern analysis) to classify distress.  On detecting a loop, it flags an alert.  It never overrides the user’s decisions and always maintains the user as the final authority【15†L69-L72】.  

- **Rescue Agent** – *Role:* Triggered by the Monitor Agent, this agent intervenes to rescue the stalled task.  
  *Goals:* Quickly gather the context of the user’s current project (code files, notes, recent edits) and generate three concrete, actionable suggestions to break the loop and resume progress.  *Capabilities:* Retrieves relevant information from the user’s recent work (using Azure AI Search) and may query external sources as needed.  It processes this information (via Code Interpreter for analysis or summarization) and produces **exactly three** independent options for how to proceed (ensuring tasks are concise and actionable)【22†L473-L476】【54†L80-L82】.  The Rescue Agent’s tone is positive and encouraging; it includes motivating feedback and never blames the user for errors【55†L205-L209】【15†L69-L72】.

- **MVP Planner Agent** – *Role:* Focus on producing a minimal viable deliverable based on the user’s project goals.  
  *Goals:* Synthesize the user’s objectives and current output into three distinct MVP plans or prototypes, each with clear steps to completion.  *Capabilities:* Uses both the user’s context (via Azure AI Search) and external knowledge (via Web Search) to inform its planning.  It employs the Code Interpreter to generate or validate drafts.  The planner outputs three high-level MVP proposals (each option is a brief outline of work to salvage the project)【22†L473-L476】【54†L80-L82】.  As with all agents, it uses constructive language and facilitates task closure, never assigning blame for the prior difficulties【55†L205-L209】.

Each agent’s responsibilities are sharply defined (monitoring, rescuing, planning) in accordance with multi-agent best practices【39†L132-L139】.  This modular design reduces complexity and allows each agent to be optimized for its function.

## System Prompts  
Each agent is initialized with a carefully crafted system prompt that defines its persona and guidelines. The prompts emphasize actionable steps, positivity, and require three suggestions per intervention.  Human oversight is explicitly enforced.  (Prompts are presented in quotes below; agents receive these as base instructions before any session.)

> **Monitor Agent System Prompt (base):**  
> “You are the **Monitor Agent**. Your job is to continuously analyze the user’s ongoing project work and behavior to detect cognitive distress (such as repetitive loops, frustration, or stalled progress). Use available data (activity logs, open files) to determine if the user is stuck. If a concerning pattern emerges, set `distressDetected = true` and escalate by invoking the Rescue Agent. Speak in a calm, professional manner; do not accuse or blame the user for any issue【15†L69-L72】【55†L205-L209】.  After analysis, output a JSON status (see schema) to indicate whether intervention is needed.”

> **Rescue Agent System Prompt (base):**  
> “You are the **Rescue Agent**, activated when a user is detected to be in distress. First, gather context about the user’s recent work (search project files and notes from the past days). Then produce **three** concrete suggestions or next steps to help complete the current task. Each suggestion must be immediately actionable and concise【22†L473-L476】. Use a supportive tone and include at least one positive reinforcement or motivating statement. Format your output as a JSON list of three options and include `"requiresApproval": true`. Do not attempt to finalize any changes without explicit human approval【15†L69-L72】. Always keep the user in control of decisions.”

> **MVP Planner Agent System Prompt (base):**  
> “You are the **MVP Planner Agent**, responsible for outlining minimal viable solutions. Analyze the collected project information and propose **three** distinct MVP plans that achieve the user’s goal with as little scope as possible. Each plan should be a summary of steps or features that can be implemented quickly. Ensure each option is clear, actionable, and user-friendly. Present your output as a JSON object listing three options. Do not take any irreversible action; set `"requiresApproval": true` on your output so that the human user can review and select an option.”

These prompts encode the requirements that each intervention yields exactly three options (consistent with design guidance to avoid user indecision by limiting choices【22†L473-L476】) and that no agent action is finalized without human sign-off【15†L69-L72】.

## Tools Assignments  
The agents leverage Azure AI Foundry’s integrated tools for enhanced capabilities.  Per Azure AI Agent Service documentation, built-in tools include **Azure AI Search**, **Code Interpreter**, and Bing/Web search【58†L121-L124】【57†L328-L330】.  We allocate them as follows:

- **Monitor Agent:** Uses the **Code Interpreter** tool for analyzing user activity logs and performing any on-the-fly computation or pattern recognition on the client environment【57†L328-L330】. (It may also be configured with Azure Functions triggers to respond to user activity events【53†L55-L59】.) It does not initiate any external web queries on its own.

- **Rescue Agent:** Uses **Azure AI Search** to query the user’s project files, documentation, and notes (an enterprise knowledge retrieval system for RAG scenarios【28†L679-L684】). It also uses the **Code Interpreter** to process or transform any retrieved content (e.g. parsing code or summarizing text). If relevant, it can use **Web Search (Bing)** to look up external examples or references for the user’s task (since Azure Agent Service includes Bing as a data source【58†L121-L124】【58†L140-L142】).

- **MVP Planner Agent:** Uses **Azure AI Search** to aggregate user-provided content and project data, the **Code Interpreter** to compute and format potential solutions, and **Web Search (Bing)** to supplement with relevant general knowledge or templates.  (All agents could also access Azure Functions if needed for event triggers【53†L55-L59】, but the main coordination is through Azure AI Search and Code Interpreter.)

By assigning tools in this way, each agent can efficiently obtain the necessary data and perform computations.  (Azure AI Agent Service documentation confirms that agents can call Bing and Azure AI Search tools seamlessly【58†L121-L124】【28†L679-L684】.)

## Agent Workflows  
The multi-agent workflow is orchestrated by event triggers and internal messaging.  We assume use of Azure Agent Service orchestration (Semantic Kernel or AutoGen) to coordinate these steps【9†L341-L349】【39†L141-L149】.  The sequence is as follows:

1. **Normal Operation:** The Monitor Agent continuously runs (for example, as an Azure Function triggered on user input events or periodic checks【53†L55-L59】).  It consumes input (see schemas) and checks for distress conditions.
2. **Trigger:** If the Monitor Agent detects a distress pattern (e.g. the same error repeated multiple times or no new output after a long duration), it outputs a “distress detected” signal.  This triggers the Rescue Agent to activate.
3. **Rescue Processing:** The Rescue Agent collects contextual data (calling Azure AI Search on recent files, code, notes) and analyzes it.  It then generates three suggested actions as described in its persona.  It outputs these suggestions in a structured JSON response, marking them as requiring human approval.
4. **MVP Planning:** Upon or after Rescue Agent’s work, the MVP Planner Agent uses the same context (and possibly the Rescue Agent’s suggestions) to formulate three MVP plans.  It also outputs JSON with three options (set `"requiresApproval": true`).
5. **Human Review:** The combined output (three suggestions from Rescue and three MVP plans) is presented to the user or a human supervisor.  The user or supervisor examines the options.  The system **will not** execute or finalize any option until the user explicitly approves one【15†L69-L72】【58†L153-L161】.
6. **Finalization:** After approval, the chosen plan may be handed off for execution or documentation (e.g. through another agent or process).  The workflow ends or resets for the next monitoring cycle.

This workflow ensures clear communication channels between agents【39†L135-L143】 and enforces the human-in-the-loop safeguard: no autonomous action is taken without explicit user consent, preserving trust【15†L69-L72】. The structured sequence and retries (e.g. only proceeding on valid approval) serve as recovery points if something goes wrong.

## Input and Output Schemas  
All agent requests and responses use precise JSON schemas to ensure deterministic parsing and interoperability (following Azure’s thread/message format【58†L119-L124】). Below are the key fields for each agent:

- **Monitor Agent**  
  - *Input (JSON):*  
    ```json
    {
      "userId": "string", 
      "activityLog": [
        {"timestamp": "ISO8601 string", "action": "description", "result": "details"}, 
        ...
      ], 
      "workspaceFiles": ["file1", "file2", ...] 
    }
    ```  
  - *Output (JSON):*  
    ```json
    {
      "distressDetected": true|false,
      "reason": "optional explanation string",
      "timestamp": "ISO8601",
      "invokeAgent": "RescueAgent"  // present if distressDetected=true
    }
    ```  
    If `distressDetected` is true, the Monitor Agent’s output signals the Rescue Agent to start.

- **Rescue Agent**  
  - *Input (JSON):*  
    ```json
    {
      "triggerId": "<id from Monitor>",
      "userId": "string",
      "recentWork": ["fileA content", "fileB content", ...],
      "context": {"currentTask": "description"}
    }
    ```  
  - *Output (JSON):*  
    ```json
    {
      "suggestions": [
        {"id": 1, "description": "Actionable suggestion text", "steps": ["step1", "step2", ...]},
        {"id": 2, "description": "Another suggestion", "steps": ["step1", "step2"]},
        {"id": 3, "description": "Third suggestion", "steps": ["step1", "step2"]}
      ],
      "requiresApproval": true
    }
    ```  
    The `requiresApproval` flag ensures human review before any action is taken. Each suggestion is immediately actionable (e.g. “Refactor function X by doing Y” or “Try approach Z next”).

- **MVP Planner Agent**  
  - *Input (JSON):*  
    ```json
    {
      "userId": "string",
      "projectSummary": "text or object summarizing goals",
      "availableResources": ["list of tools/constraints"]
    }
    ```  
  - *Output (JSON):*  
    ```json
    {
      "mvpOptions": [
        {"id": 1, "plan": "Outline of MVP plan A", "milestones": ["Milestone1", "Milestone2", ...]},
        {"id": 2, "plan": "Outline of MVP plan B", "milestones": ["..."]},
        {"id": 3, "plan": "Outline of MVP plan C", "milestones": ["..."]}
      ],
      "requiresApproval": true
    }
    ```  

Using fixed JSON fields and types prevents ambiguity. (Azure AI Agent Service handles threads of such JSON messages between agents【58†L119-L124】.)  All outputs include explicit next steps to reduce cognitive load【22†L473-L476】 and a clear approval requirement to close the loop.

## Error Handling and Recovery  
The agents include robust fallback behaviors and align with responsible AI practices【58†L153-L161】【9†L352-L360】:

- If **tool calls fail** (e.g. Azure AI Search returns no result or Code Interpreter raises an exception), the agent logs the error to the central monitoring system and retries once.  If it still fails, the agent issues a safe fallback: for example, the Rescue Agent may output a generic supportive suggestion (“Consider taking a short break and then review the steps”) instead of three specific options.  In all cases, the agent must never terminate abruptly or crash; it falls back to a “do nothing” safe state if unsure.

- If any **agent’s output is potentially harmful or irrelevant**, content filtering is enforced.  Azure’s built-in content safety filters (as part of Foundry) screen the suggestions; if flagged, the agent discards that output and either generates a neutral message or escalates to a human.  This follows Azure’s guidance to use content filters and responsible AI safeguards【58†L153-L161】.

- The user can **pause or disable** interventions. Consistent with inclusive design recommendations, the system allows the user to temporarily disable pop-up interventions (e.g. an “agent off” setting)【22†L470-L476】. This prevents repeated interruptions that could cause anxiety (users have reported that unexpected pop-ups can “crash” their flow【22†L489-L492】). The agent will respect this setting and suspend alerts until re-enabled.

- In all error messages or queries to the user, the language is neutral and non-judgmental.  The system never blames the user for errors【55†L205-L209】; if clarification is needed, the agent asks polite questions (e.g. “Can you clarify what you were trying to do here?”).

- Azure Foundry’s observability features (telemetry, logging, tracing) are used to detect any anomalous behavior【9†L352-L360】.  Additionally, an AI **Red Teaming** process periodically tests the agents against safety benchmarks【9†L372-L381】.  Any identified issues are tracked over time. These measures ensure continuous improvement and quick recovery if an agent exhibits a failure mode.

Overall, by requiring explicit user confirmation before actions and by integrating Azure’s monitoring and safety tooling, the system maintains a fail-safe human-in-the-loop model【15†L69-L72】【58†L153-L161】. All errors are handled gracefully with transparency, preserving trust and allowing the user to remain in control.  

**Sources:** This design follows Azure AI Foundry best practices for multi-agent orchestration【9†L341-L349】【39†L135-L143】【58†L121-L124】 and Microsoft’s Inclusive Design recommendations for cognitive support【15†L69-L72】【54†L80-L82】【55†L205-L209】【22†L473-L476】, ensuring a professional, user-centered solution.

# UICare Agent Orchestration

The UICare system is orchestrated exclusively by **Microsoft Semantic Kernel (SK)**, which serves as the deterministic backbone for managing all AI agents and their interactions【23†L308-L317】【60†L60-L68】. In our design, three specialized agents collaborate under SK’s control:

- **MonitorAgent** – Continuously assesses the user’s state and project context (detecting obsessive-compulsive loops or “mania” in the user’s workflow).  
- **RescueAgent** – Analyzes the user’s recent work and current project status, summarizes context, and generates intermediate guidance to steer the user back on track.  
- **MVPPlannerAgent** – Constructs concrete solution plans (minimal viable product proposals) by synthesizing insights from the Monitor and Rescue agents; it then proposes concrete next steps or options.

Each agent is implemented as an SK *skill* (a module of one or more semantic functions), and SK’s **Planner** coordinates their execution.  This architecture leverages SK’s agent framework (released 2024–2025) to create a structured, multi-agent workflow【23†L308-L317】【58†L60-L68】. Importantly, we configure SK with zero-temperature (deterministic) settings and fixed prompt templates to ensure repeatable outcomes. 

## Semantic Kernel Architecture

The core of orchestration is an SK `Kernel` (DI container) configured with Azure AI services. We deploy our LLMs via **Azure AI Foundry** (model catalog) and connect them to SK using the `AzureAIInferenceChatCompletion` connector【49†L104-L113】. For example, in SK Python:

```python
from semantic_kernel import Kernel
from semantic_kernel.connectors.ai.azure_ai_inference import AzureAIInferenceChatCompletion

kernel = Kernel.builder().configure(lambda cfg: 
    cfg.add_ai_service(AzureAIInferenceChatCompletion(ai_model_id="<deployment-name>"))
).build()
```

This binds SK to our Azure-hosted model (“deployment-name”) by reading the Foundry endpoint and key from environment variables【49†L104-L113】【12†L49-L58】. All agents share this kernel, ensuring consistent model usage. We set the model `temperature=0.0` in SK’s prompt execution settings to guarantee deterministic function-calling behavior. 

Once initialized, we **import each agent skill** into the kernel.  For example, in C# one might write:

```csharp
Kernel kernel = new KernelBuilder()
    .AddAzureOpenAIChatCompletion(deploymentName, endpoint, apiKey)
    .Build();

// Import skills for each agent:
var monitorSkill = kernel.ImportSkill(new MonitorAgentSkill(), "Monitor");
var rescueSkill  = kernel.ImportSkill(new RescueAgentSkill(),  "Rescue");
var mvpSkill     = kernel.ImportSkill(new MVPPlannerSkill(), "MVP");
```

Each skill class contains one or more SK functions (marked with `[SKFunction]` or `@kernel_function`) that encapsulate the agent’s capabilities.  For example:

- **MonitorAgentSkill** might define functions like `CheckManiaStatus(context)` (a function that inspects recent user activity and returns a label or score) and `RetrieveRecentWork(logs)` (pulling in recent project data).  
- **RescueAgentSkill** might define `SummarizeProject(context)`, `GenerateGuidance(summary)`, etc., each implemented via semantic prompts.  
- **MVPPlannerSkill** might define `ProposeMVPOptions(context)` (returning a list of minimal solutions) and `PrioritizeOptions(options)`.  

These SK functions use the LLM to process information: for example, `CheckManiaStatus` could prompt the model with the user’s recent code edits or chat logs and ask it to detect repeating patterns or frustration signals. All such semantic functions are deterministic given our fixed prompts and zero temperature. This **skills-and-functions structure** creates a clear separation of concerns among agents.

## SK Planner and Deterministic Workflow

To coordinate the agents, we use SK’s **agent orchestration framework**. Specifically, we create an agent “executor” (e.g. `KernelFunctionOrchestrationEngine` in .NET or an equivalent orchestration loop in Python) with:

- A **termination strategy** (`KernelFunctionTerminationStrategy`) that uses an LLM function to decide when to stop the loop【42†L338-L347】【60†L60-L68】. For UICare, we define this via a prompt function such as:

    > *“Determine if the user’s project has been stabilized or an MVP is approved. If yes, respond with ‘yes’.”*  

  In code, this uses `KernelFunctionFactory.CreateFromPrompt()` to build a `KernelFunction` that inspects the conversation history and outputs “yes” only when the rescue/plan is complete【42†L338-L347】. This ensures the agent conversation ends explicitly when the stopping condition is met.  

- A **selection strategy** (`KernelFunctionSelectionStrategy`) that controls which agent speaks next【42†L392-L401】【58†L60-L68】. We use a simple sequential or prompt-driven strategy: after Monitor goes, Rescue goes, then MVPPlanner, then back to Monitor, etc.  For example, the prompt might list agent names and turn-taking rules, and the LLM returns the next agent name【42†L398-L407】. By fixing this logic via a SK `KernelFunction`, we guarantee a deterministic turn order (Monitor → Rescue → MVP → repeat) until termination. 

Concretely, the orchestration loop looks like this:

1. **MonitorAgent turn:** SK invokes `monitorSkill.CheckManiaStatus` (passing user context). If mania detected, proceed; else terminate.  
2. **RescueAgent turn:** SK invokes `rescueSkill.SummarizeProject` on the gathered context, then `rescueSkill.GenerateGuidance`. It posts intermediate suggestions to the user.  
3. **MVPPlannerAgent turn:** SK invokes `mvpSkill.ProposeMVPOptions` and `mvpSkill.PrioritizeOptions` to create actionable plans.  
4. **Loop/Termination:** The `KernelFunctionTerminationStrategy` checks if the user’s state is now stable or an MVP was found; if not, return to step 1.

The SK orchestration classes (`KernelFunctionSelectionStrategy`, `KernelFunctionTerminationStrategy`) handle passing conversation state (the shared “chat history” or variables) between agents【58†L60-L68】【60†L60-L68】. We configure a strict maximum iteration count (e.g. 5) as a safety guard on the termination strategy to enforce deterministic bounds. 

All SK pipeline steps are fully specified: each function is invoked in order by the planner, and any branching logic is encoded in prompts. There is no randomness. By using function-calling and prompt templates, the plan is **reproducible every time** the same input is given. (For instance, we record the user’s context in a vector store or SK memory and pass it deterministically to the MonitorAgent functions.)

## Azure AI Foundry Integration

UICare’s LLM and tools integrate with Azure AI Foundry in two ways:

- **Model Deployment:** We host the LLM (e.g. GPT-4o, Claude 3, or Mistral) and any embedding models on Azure AI Foundry.  SK connects to these via the `AzureAIInferenceChatCompletion` (for chat completions) and `AzureAIInferenceTextEmbedding` (for vector embedding lookups) classes【49†L104-L113】. Environment variables (or Azure Managed Identity) handle authentication, per Microsoft’s guidelines【49†L104-L113】【12†L49-L58】. 

- **Semantic Kernel Foundry Bindings:** We follow Microsoft’s 2025 best practices by treating Foundry models as SK AI service plugins, not writing custom OpenAI calls.  All LLM invocations go through SK’s connectors. For example, setting `kernel.Configuration.LanguageModel = "model-id-in-foundry"` uses the Foundry LLM. This aligns with the official guidance to use Azure AI Foundry for enterprise-grade deployment【12†L49-L58】. 

This binding allows us to update models or deploy new ones via the Azure portal without changing the SK code – we only supply the new Foundry `deploymentName` and endpoint. 

## Final Blueprint and Code Structure

In summary, the **exact SK components** are:

- **Skills & Functions:** Three skill classes (`MonitorAgentSkill`, `RescueAgentSkill`, `MVPPlannerSkill`), each with semantic functions implementing the agent’s subtasks (via system prompt templates). These are registered with the Kernel.  

- **Planner/Orchestration:** An SK *agent executor* configured with a `KernelFunctionSelectionStrategy` and `KernelFunctionTerminationStrategy`. The selection function is implemented by a prompt that deterministically names which agent goes next【42†L398-L407】【58†L60-L68】. The termination function is implemented by a prompt that ends the loop when the job is done【42†L338-L347】【60†L60-L68】. We instantiate this, for example, in C# as:
  ```csharp
  // Pseudocode for orchestration engine setup
  KernelFunction terminateFn = KernelFunctionFactory.CreateFromPrompt("if stabilized, answer 'yes'. History: {{$history}}");
  var terminationStrategy = new KernelFunctionTerminationStrategy(terminateFn, kernel);

  KernelFunction selectFn = KernelFunctionFactory.CreateFromPrompt("Who goes next? History: {{$history}} ...");
  var selectionStrategy = new KernelFunctionSelectionStrategy(selectFn, kernel);

  var orchestration = new KernelFunctionOrchestrationEngine(
      agents: new[]{monitorAgent, rescueAgent, mvpAgent},
      selectionStrategy, terminationStrategy);

  await orchestration.RunAsync(initialUserInput);
  ```
  Each `agent` above is defined by its skill and system prompt.  

- **Deterministic Settings:** We explicitly set SK’s prompt execution settings (max tokens, temperature=0, fixed top_p, etc.) to eliminate nondeterminism【21†L49-L58】. All selection/termination logic is rule-based (via prompt functions), so the loop is fully predictable.

- **Logging & Observability:** Following 2025 best practices, SK emits telemetry (traces, token usage) via its built-in observability features【12†L49-L58】. We instrument key events (agent transitions, function calls) so the team can audit the orchestration flow in production.

By using Semantic Kernel **solely** as the orchestrator, we achieve a clean, modular design. Each agent’s logic is encapsulated in its SK skill, and the SK planner deterministically sequences their invocation. Azure AI Foundry hosts the models, and SK’s connectors seamlessly integrate them. The result is a production-grade, fully deterministic multi-agent workflow: a reproducible “blueprint” where every planner step, skill function, and loop condition is explicitly defined and governed by SK’s agent framework【23†L308-L317】【58†L60-L68】.

**Sources:** This design follows Microsoft’s 2024–2025 guidance on Semantic Kernel multi-agent orchestration and Azure AI Agent Service integration【23†L308-L317】【12†L49-L58】【58†L60-L68】. All components above use official SK APIs and Azure AI Foundry bindings as documented.

# UICare Project Plan: CI/CD Pipeline and Documentation

## 8. Continuous Integration and Deployment (CI/CD)  
The UICare project will use **GitHub Actions** for a robust, production-grade CI/CD pipeline.  We will define two workflows: a **CI workflow** (`.github/workflows/ci.yml`) that runs on every push or pull request, and a **CD workflow** (`.github/workflows/cd.yml`) that runs on pushes to the main branch.  Both workflows run on **Ubuntu runners** for consistency.  The CI workflow will **checkout** the code, use `actions/setup-node@v3` to install Node.js (or appropriate runtimes) and automatically cache dependencies【27†L331-L339】.  We will enable caching (via `actions/cache` or builtin setup actions) to speed up `npm ci` or `pip install`.  Typical CI steps include linting, building each module (e.g. `/src/extension`, `/src/backend`, `/src/orchestration`), and running unit tests.  Each job uses small steps with clear logging.  For example, the CI job might include:
- `uses: actions/checkout@v3` to fetch code.
- `uses: actions/setup-node@v3` with `cache: 'npm'` to install Node.js and cache `node_modules`【27†L331-L339】.
- Shell commands `npm install`, `npm run build`, `npm test` (with proper exit codes on failure).
- **Artifacts and badges**: At the end of CI, collect test results or coverage reports as GitHub Action artifacts for review.  

The **CD workflow** triggers on `push` to `main`. It first logs into Azure using a **service principal** (via `azure/login@v1`) with secrets for authentication. We will store the Azure credentials (`AZURE_CLIENT_ID`, `AZURE_TENANT_ID`, `AZURE_SUBSCRIPTION_ID`) in GitHub Secrets as recommended by Microsoft【15†L199-L207】.  (For maximum security on public repos, we will use an **environment secret** that requires reviewer approval【15†L194-L202】.) The workflow then builds and pushes Docker images for the backend/orchestration components. Using the **Azure Container Registry (ACR)** or Docker Hub, the job uses `azure/docker-login@v1` or `docker/login-action` with additional secrets (e.g. `REGISTRY_USERNAME`, `REGISTRY_PASSWORD`) to authenticate, then runs `docker build` and `docker push`. Finally, the workflow uses `azure/container-apps-deploy-action@v1` to deploy to Azure Container Apps, supplying the registry name, container app name, resource group, and image tag (typically the Git commit SHA)【23†L118-L124】【17†L113-L121】.  Example snippet from the official docs:
```yaml
- name: Azure Login
  uses: azure/login@v1
  with:
    creds: ${{ secrets.AZURE_CREDENTIALS }}
- name: Build and Deploy UICare Container App
  uses: azure/container-apps-deploy-action@v1
  with:
    acrName: <yourRegistryName>
    containerAppName: uicare-backend-app
    resourceGroup: UICareResourceGroup
    imageToDeploy: <yourRegistryName>.azurecr.io/uicare-backend:${{ github.sha }}
``` 
The deploy action will auto-build the container from the Dockerfile in `appSourcePath` (or pull the tagged image)【23†L100-L104】【23†L118-L124】.  We must ensure each service has a proper `Dockerfile`.  Note: we use the **commit SHA** in the image tag (not `latest`) to ensure immutability【23†L100-L104】.

All required **GitHub Secrets** must be configured in the repository settings (or protected environments) before deployment: in addition to the Azure credentials listed above【15†L199-L207】, we will add `FOUNDRY_CONNECTION_STRING` (the secure connection string for the Foundry service) and any ACR credentials if needed. These secrets are referenced in the Actions workflow with `${{ secrets.SECRET_NAME }}`. The CI and CD workflows will also use `actions/checkout`, and we will apply standard best practices: pinning action versions, limiting permissions, and running jobs in parallel as needed. For example, caching dependencies with `actions/setup-node` greatly speeds up CI loops【27†L331-L339】, and branch protection rules will ensure that only code passing CI can be merged to `main`. 

**Azure Deployment Target:** UICare’s components will be containerized microservices (backend API, orchestration engine, etc.), so **Azure Container Apps** is the recommended deployment target. Microsoft documentation notes that Container Apps provides “more flexibility with containers” and finer-grained scaling compared to App Service【4†L107-L115】. Container Apps runs on Kubernetes under the hood, supporting **scale-to-zero** for cost-efficiency (unlike App Service)【6†L116-L124】. Therefore, we will provision an Azure Container Apps environment (with Managed Identity enabled) and deploy each microservice as a separate Container App. This choice avoids managing VM or App Service instances directly and fits our microservices architecture【4†L107-L115】【6†L116-L124】. All resource names (e.g. `UICareResourceGroup`, `uicare-backend-app`) will be finalized in the CD workflow. In summary, the production CI/CD flows use GitHub Actions with secret-managed credentials【15†L199-L207】, container images stored in ACR, and deployment via **azure/container-apps-deploy-action** to Azure Container Apps, ensuring a scalable, maintainable pipeline.

## 9. Documentation and Setup  
We will provide a comprehensive **README.md** at the repository root (top-level), as GitHub and other platforms display it by default【28†L31-L39】. The root README will include clear sections for **Setup**, **Running**, **Testing**, and **Deployment**:

- **Setup:** List prerequisites (e.g. Node.js version, Azure CLI, ACR access). Specify required environment variables or secrets (e.g. `FOUNDRY_CONNECTION_STRING`, Azure IDs) and how to configure them (e.g. via a `.env` file or GitHub Secrets).  
- **Running:** Describe how to launch the application locally. For example, provide commands to install dependencies (`npm install`), build each module, and start services (`npm start` or `npm run dev`). If the extension is a browser plugin, include packaging instructions.  
- **Testing:** Explain how to run automated tests, linting, or type checks. For instance, `npm test` or `npm run lint` for each module. Include any requirements for generating coverage reports.  
- **Deployment:** Detail the CI/CD process. For example, note that GitHub Actions handle deployment on push to `main`, and list any manual steps if needed (like creating Azure resources via the Azure CLI or running `az containerapp up`).  
Each section will use concise step-by-step instructions or bullet lists. For instance, the Setup section might bullet out installing Node and logging into Azure CLI. We will use Markdown headings (e.g. `## Setup`) for clarity.

### Example Root README Outline  
```markdown
# UICare

## Setup
- **Prerequisites:** Node.js >=16, Docker, Azure CLI, Git.
- **Environment Variables:** Create a `.env` file (or GitHub Secrets) with:
  - `FOUNDRY_CONNECTION_STRING` = *[Obtained from Foundry portal]*
  - `AZURE_CLIENT_ID`, `AZURE_TENANT_ID`, `AZURE_SUBSCRIPTION_ID` = *[Azure service principal credentials]*.
- **Install Dependencies:** `npm ci` at repository root (which installs all modules via workspaces).

## Running Locally
1. Start the Backend API: `(cd src/backend && npm start)`.
2. Start the Extension UI (or frontend): `(cd src/extension && npm start)`.
3. Start the Orchestration Engine: `(cd src/orchestration && npm start)`.
4. The application runs on `http://localhost:3000` (or configured port).

## Testing
- **Run Tests:** For each module, run `(cd src/<module> && npm test)`. 
- **Linting:** Use `(npm run lint)` to check code style in all folders.
- **Coverage:** (If applicable) generate coverage with `npm run coverage`.

## Deployment
- **CI/CD:** On push to `main`, GitHub Actions builds and deploys images to Azure Container Apps.
- **Manual Deploy:** To deploy manually, build Docker images, push to ACR, then use `az containerapp update` with the new image tag.
- **Monitoring:** Application logs and metrics can be accessed via Azure Monitor / Application Insights.
```
This outline ensures users and developers can quickly get started. We will cite best practices for README placement and format【28†L31-L39】 to justify having a top-level Markdown README.

### Module-Level README Templates  
In addition to the root README, each module directory (`/src/extension`, `/src/backend`, `/src/orchestration`) will contain its own `README.md`. Each module README follows a consistent template:

- **Title:** The module name (e.g. `# Backend Service`) and a one-line purpose.
- **Overview:** Brief description of the module’s function (e.g. “Handles user requests and interfaces with Foundry.”).
- **Setup:** Specific installation or build steps (e.g. `npm install` inside the module folder).
- **Running/Usage:** How to run this module (commands, environment variables, endpoints).
- **Testing:** Module-specific tests or demo commands.
- **Configuration:** Module-specific config (e.g. ports, database URLs, API keys).
- **Dependencies:** Key libraries or services used.
  
For example, `/src/backend/README.md` might look like:

```markdown
# UICare Backend Service

**Overview:** The backend API service provides REST endpoints for the UICare application, connecting the front-end extension to the Foundry data service.

**Setup:**  
- Requires Node.js >=16.  
- Install dependencies: `npm install`.  

**Running:**  
- Start the server: `npm start`.  
- By default it listens on `http://localhost:4000` (configurable via `PORT`).  

**Configuration:**  
- Reads `FOUNDRY_CONNECTION_STRING` (set from root `.env`).  
- Other env vars: `DATABASE_URL`, `API_KEY`, etc.  

**Testing:**  
- Run `npm test` to execute unit tests.  

**Dependencies:**  
- Express.js for the web server.  
- Axios for HTTP calls to Foundry.  
- Jest for testing.
```

Similar README templates will be created for `/src/extension` and `/src/orchestration`, following the same structure (Overview, Setup, Running, Config, Testing). This ensures each component is self-describing and easy to develop or debug independently.

Overall, these sections provide **final, unambiguous recommendations** for CI/CD and documentation. All GitHub workflows will be implemented as described, secrets will be explicitly named and used, Azure Container Apps is chosen definitively as the deployment target【4†L107-L115】【6†L116-L124】, and the README files will contain all setup/run/test/deploy instructions needed. The result will match professional open-source standards with thorough guidance for developers and users.  

**Sources:** Official Azure and GitHub documentation were used to inform CI/CD best practices and deployment choices【4†L107-L115】【6†L116-L124】【15†L199-L207】【23†L118-L124】【27†L331-L339】【28†L31-L39】. These ensure our plan is grounded in current best practices.

from reportlab.lib.pagesizes import letter
from reportlab.platypus import SimpleDocTemplate, Paragraph, Spacer
from reportlab.lib.styles import getSampleStyleSheet

# Reconstructed canvas content
canvas_content = """
# Market Opportunity Analysis for UICare

UICare is envisioned as an AI-driven assistant that monitors a user's activity across applications to detect when they are stuck in unproductive loops or sabotaging their progress, and then intervenes to help. This analysis examines the potential market for UICare and how to position it: either as part of a specialized PRD builder app, split into two apps (a PRD tool and a general UICare assistant), or as a system-wide productivity and well-being assistant. We identify target user segments, pain points, existing solutions, gaps in the market, and strategic recommendations.

---

## 1. Target User Segments and Pain Points

**Developers & Engineers**
- Struggle with debugging/perfection loops (“yak shaving”).

**Writers & Content Creators**
- Prone to writer’s block, obsessively refining single sentences.

**Product Managers & Knowledge Workers**
- Overwhelmed by complex documents, endless reorganizing (“decision distress”).

**Designers & Creative Professionals**
- Endless undo/redo cycles in design tools.

**Neurodivergent Individuals (ADHD, Bipolar, ASD)**
- Experience hyper-focus swings and obsessive loops.

**Cross-Application Pain Patterns**
- Frequent app switching (13 apps, 30 switches/day) harms productivity.

---

## 2. Existing Solutions

Focus timers, productivity trackers, AI copilots, and mental health-aware tools address parts of the problem but leave gaps.

---

## 3. Market Gaps

- No cross-app contextual monitoring.
- Lack of proactive intervention.
- Limited mental health integration.
- Low personalization.

---

## 4. Strategic Positioning Options

### Option 1: Embedded in a PRD Builder App
- Pros: Focused use case.
- Cons: Limited market, user perception.

### Option 2: Two Separate Apps
- Pros: Tailored experiences.
- Cons: Resource dilution, brand confusion.

### Option 3: Standalone Cross-Application Assistant
- Pros: Broad market, category-defining.
- Cons: Technical complexity, privacy challenges.

---

## Recommendation

Position UICare as a standalone, cross-application AI assistant with phased rollout starting in a specific domain, then expand.

---

# Next Deliverable: UICare VS Code Extension POC Implementation Plan

1. Define
   - **Problem:** Implement UICare’s core rescue in VS Code when a developer loops in editing.
   - **Desired Output:** Functional VS Code extension scaffolding monitoring file edits and invoking AI suggestions.

2. Explore
   - **initial_thoughts:** Leverage VS Code Extension API
   - **analysis:** Extension must capture document events and call AI service
   - **alternatives:**
     1. VS Code extension (promising)
     2. TS language server plugin (explore)
     3. External desktop app (unsuitable)
   - **evaluation:**
     1. promising (full API access)
     2. explore (higher complexity)
     3. unsuitable (OS hooks heavy)

3. Refine
   - **selected_path:** VS Code Extension
   - **reasoning:** Full editor API, fast proof-of-concept, clear demo
   - **critical_analysis:** Requires packing AI key securely
   - **refinement:** Focus on document change events and AI call flow

4. Present
   - **final_solution:** Scaffold extension using `yo code`, monitor edits, trigger AI calls, display Webview with three options
   - **SWO:** Strengths: fast POC; Weaknesses: limited to code; Opportunities: developer advocates

---

## Next Steps: Microsoft AI Agents Platform & GitHub Setup

1. **Select Microsoft Agent Framework**
   - Use Azure AI Agents Service (preview).

2. **Transition Timing**
   - Complete all documentation before scaffolding Agents Project.

3. **Step-by-Step Agent Platform Integration**
   a. Install Azure CLI extension: `az extension add --name agent-service-preview`
   b. Create Agents Workspace: `az agent create --name UICareAgents --resource-group <YourResourceGroup> --location <YourRegion>`
   c. Define agent pipelines in `agents-config.json`.
   d. Deploy agent project: `az agent deploy --workspace UICareAgents --project-path ./agents`
   e. Configure Azure OpenAI endpoints in Agent Workspace settings.

4. **GitHub Repository Setup**
   - `git init`, set `.gitignore`, commit initial code.
   - `gh repo create coreyalejandro/uicare --private`, push `main`.
   - Add GitHub Actions for linting, tests, and Azure deployment.
"""

# Create PDF
pdf_path = "/mnt/data/uicare_canvas.pdf"
doc = SimpleDocTemplate(pdf_path, pagesize=letter)
styles = getSampleStyleSheet()
story = []
for line in canvas_content.split("\n\n"):
    if line.startswith("# "):
        story.append(Paragraph(line, styles['Heading1']))
    elif line.startswith("## "):
        story.append(Paragraph(line, styles['Heading2']))
    else:
        story.append(Paragraph(line.replace("\n", "<br/>"), styles['BodyText']))
    story.append(Spacer(1, 12))
doc.build(story)

That's not the complete Canvas. This is:
<complete_canvas>
# Market Opportunity Analysis for UICare - ***vers 11***

UICare is envisioned as an AI-driven assistant that monitors a user's activity across applications to detect when they are stuck in unproductive loops or sabotaging their progress, and then intervenes to help. This analysis examines the potential market for UICare and how to position it: either as part of a specialized PRD builder app, split into two apps (a PRD tool and a general UICare assistant), or as a system-wide productivity and well-being assistant. We identify target user segments, pain points, existing solutions, gaps in the market, and strategic recommendations.

---

## 1. Target User Segments and Pain Points

**Developers & Engineers**
- Struggle with debugging/perfection loops (“yak shaving”).
- Endless tweaking leads to frustration, wasted hours, missed deadlines.

**Writers & Content Creators**
- Prone to writer’s block, obsessively refining single sentences (e.g., spending a whole day on three words).
- Leads to analysis paralysis and diminished creativity.

**Product Managers & Knowledge Workers**
- Overwhelmed by complex documents, endless reorganizing (“decision distress” — 85% admit second-guessing decisions).
- Looping between task trackers, spreadsheets, docs without real progress.

**Designers & Creative Professionals**
- Endless undo/redo cycles in design tools, perfectionism stalls progress.

**Neurodivergent Individuals (ADHD, Bipolar, ASD)**
- Experience hyper-focus swings, obsessive loops, difficulty self-regulating under pressure.

**Cross-Application Pain Patterns**
- Frequent app switching (13 apps, 30 switches/day) harms productivity.
- Difficulty tracking context across silos; duplicate efforts.

---

## 2. Existing Solutions

**Focus Timers (Pomodoro Apps)**
- Tools like Focus Booster, Forest structure work into intervals.
- Help with time management but cannot detect lack of actual progress.

**Productivity Trackers & Distraction Blockers**
- RescueTime logs app usage, categorizes productivity, offers Focus Sessions.
- Insightful reports but passive; no proactive help when actually stuck.

**AI Copilots & In-App Assistants**
- GitHub Copilot, Notion AI help on user request within a single app.
- Do not sense emotional state or cross-app context; always user-initiated.

**Well-Being & Mental Health Tools**
- Microsoft Viva Insights, Digital Wellbeing reminders encourage breaks.
- Therapeutic chatbots (Wysa, Woebot) separate from work context; not proactive in-app assistance.

---

## 3. Market Gaps

- **No Cross-App Contextual Monitoring:** No tool combines system-level monitoring with content awareness across multiple apps.
- **Lack of Proactive Intervention:** Existing tools provide data or reminders, not smart, timely rescue when loops begin.
- **Limited Mental Health Integration:** Productivity vs. well-being tools remain siloed; none bridge emotional state and work output.
- **Low Personalization:** Few tools adapt to an individual’s unique patterns or preemptively assist before crisis.

---

## 4. Strategic Positioning Options

### Option 1: Embedded in a PRD Builder App
- **Pros:** Focused use case, simpler integration, niche differentiation.
- **Cons:** Limited market (PRD writers only), risks overfitting and missing broader demand.

### Option 2: Two Separate Apps (PRD Tool + General UICare)
- **Pros:** Tailored experiences, cross-promotion, dedicated development tracks.
- **Cons:** Resource dilution, brand confusion, complex integration.

### Option 3: Standalone Cross-Application Assistant
- **Pros:** Broad addressable market, category-defining potential, synergy with existing AI copilots, network effects.
- **Cons:** Technical complexity (OS/app integration), privacy/trust challenges, potential platform competition.

---

## 5. Recommendation

**Launch UICare as a standalone, cross-application AI assistant**, with an optional focused rollout (e.g., IDE companion). This maximizes addressable market, leverages a unique value proposition (proactive, context-aware rescue), and positions UICare as a category leader. A phased approach—starting with one domain—mitigates technical risk while preserving the system-wide vision.

**Next Steps:**
1. Prototype a proof-of-concept for one application domain (e.g., VS Code extension).
2. Validate detection algorithms with real users.
3. Develop core UI/UX flows and privacy safeguards.
4. Expand integrations to other key apps (Word, Figma, Excel).  
5. Launch beta and iterate based on user feedback.

---

*Stored for future reference and continuous iteration.*

---

# Next Deliverable: UICare VS Code Extension POC Implementation Plan

To begin implementing the recommended standalone, cross-application assistant, we will prototype UICare as a **Visual Studio Code extension**. VS Code is a high-impact domain for developers, easy to extend, and allows rapid demonstration of core UICare features: loop detection, context snapshot, and AI-driven rescue suggestions.

## 1. Define  
**Problem:** Implement UICare’s core rescue in VS Code when a developer loops in editing.  
**Desired Output:** Functional VS Code extension scaffolding monitoring file edits and invoking AI suggestions.

## 2. Explore  
- **initial_thoughts:** Leverage VS Code Extension API  
- **analysis:** Extension must capture document events and call AI service  
- **alternatives:**  
  1. VS Code extension (recommended)  
  2. TS language server plugin  
  3. External desktop app hooking VS Code  
- **evaluation:**  
  1. promising (full API access)  
  2. explore (higher complexity)  
  3. unsuitable (OS hooks heavy)

## 3. Refine  
**selected_path:** VS Code Extension  
**reasoning:** Full editor API, fast proof-of-concept, clear demo  
**critical_analysis:** Requires packing AI key securely  
**refinement:** Focus on document change events and AI call flow

## 4. Present  
**final_solution:**  
1. **Scaffold Extension:** 
   - `yo code` (TypeScript) template.  
   - Activate on `onLanguage:javascript,typescript` and `*` events.  
2. **Monitor Edits:** 
   - Use `workspace.onDidChangeTextDocument` to track changes.  
   - Record timestamp, diff size, and undo/redo counts.  
   - Implement simple heuristics (e.g., >5 edits in 2 min on same line).  
3. **Trigger Rescue:** 
   - When heuristic met, snapshot document text.  
   - Send snapshot + simple prompt to AI API (Azure OpenAI).  
   - Stream response via `window.showInformationMessage` or custom Webview panel.  
4. **Rescue UI:** 
   - Create Webview panel with three tabs (“Option 1”, “Option 2”, “Option 3”).  
   - Each tab displays AI-generated code suggestion.  
   - “Apply” button injects code into editor at snapshot range.  
5. **Security & Configuration:**  
   - Store API key in VS Code `settings.json` under `uicare.apiKey`.  
   - Provide setting for sensitivity thresholds.  
6. **Testing & Demo:**  
   - Write unit tests for heuristic logic.  
   - Demo with a sample JavaScript file, intentionally loop edits.  
   - Validate that rescue panel appears and AI suggestions load.  

**SWO:**  
- **Strengths:** Fast POC, clear demo, leverages existing ecosystems  
- **Weaknesses:** Limited to code; needs expansion for other apps  
- **Opportunities:** Show power in one domain, attract developer advocates  

---

Reply **Ready** when you’d like the scaffolded extension code delivered.

## Next Steps: Microsoft AI Agents Platform & GitHub Setup

1. **Select Microsoft Agent Framework**  
   - We'll use the **Azure AI Agents Service** (preview) aligned with hackathon rules. This platform supports multi-agent orchestration, tool integrations, and Azure OpenAI models out of the box.

2. **Transition Timing**  
   - Complete all documentation (technical plan, API definitions, prompt templates, UX flows) first.  
   - Then scaffold an **Agents Project** in Azure: use the Azure AI Agents CLI to generate agent manifest files reflecting UICare’s agents (Context Analyst, Planner, Implementer Agents, Critic).  
   - Implement each agent’s logic by wiring your Node.js/TypeScript code (or Python AutoGen modules) into the Azure Agent orchestration runtime.

3. **Step-by-Step Agent Platform Integration**  
   - **a.** Install Azure CLI and the AI Agents extension:  
     ```bash
     az extension add --name agent-service-preview
     ```  
   - **b.** Create a new Agents Workspace:  
     ```bash
     az agent create \
       --name UICareAgents \
       --resource-group <YourResourceGroup> \
       --location <YourRegion>
     ```  
   - **c.** Define agent pipelines in `agents-config.json`: list agents, prompts, tools, and triggers (matching UICare roles).  
   - **d.** Deploy the agent project:  
     ```bash
     az agent deploy --workspace UICareAgents --project-path ./agents
     ```  
   - **e.** Configure Azure OpenAI model endpoints and keys in the Agent Workspace settings for GPT-4.

4. **GitHub Repository Setup**  
   - Initialize a Git repository at the project root:
     ```bash
     git init
     echo "node_modules/
.vscode/
.env" > .gitignore
     git add .
     git commit -m "Initial UICare VS Code Extension & AI Agents plan"
     ```  
   - Create a GitHub repo (via CLI or web) and push:
     ```bash
     gh repo create coreyalejandro/uicare --private
     git push -u origin main
     ```  
   - Add CI workflows (GitHub Actions) to automate linting, tests, and Azure deployment.

By following these steps, you’ll have complete documentation, a proven VS Code extension prototype, and a fully scaffolded Azure AI Agents solution ready for handoff to automated agents to build and deploy UICare at hackathon standards.

### 4. CI Workflow

```yaml
name: CI
on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
  pull_request:
    branches: [main]
jobs:
  build-and-test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    strategy:
      matrix:
        project: [extension, backend, orchestration, agents]
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v3
      - name: Setup Node.js
        if: "matrix.project == 'extension' || matrix.project == 'backend'"
        uses: actions/setup-node@v3
        with:
          node-version: '18'
      - name: Install Dependencies
        working-directory: src/${{ matrix.project }}
        run: npm ci
      - name: Build
        working-directory: src/${{ matrix.project }}
        run: npm run build
      - name: Run Tests
        working-directory: src/${{ matrix.project }}
        run: npm test
```

This scaffold ensures immediate code commitments can follow a consistent structure with CI validation. You can now begin populating each folder with module code and documentation.

## 5. VS Code Extension Full Scaffolding

We have initialized the extension folder. Next, populate key files:

### 5.4 src/extension/src/extension.ts
```ts
import * as vscode from 'vscode';
import { askAgentCommand } from './commands/askAgent';

export function activate(context: vscode.ExtensionContext) {
  context.subscriptions.push(
    vscode.commands.registerCommand('uicare.askAgent', askAgentCommand)
  );
}

export function deactivate() {}
```

### 5.5 src/extension/src/commands/askAgent.ts
```ts
import * as vscode from 'vscode';
import { AgentApiClient } from '../services/AgentApiClient';

export async function askAgentCommand() {
  const prompt = await vscode.window.showInputBox({ prompt: 'Describe your issue or task' });
  if (!prompt) { return; }
  const response = await AgentApiClient.sendPrompt(prompt);
  ChatView.showResponse(response);
}
```

### 5.6 src/extension/src/services/AgentApiClient.ts
```ts
import { DefaultAzureCredential } from '@azure/identity';
import { AIProjectsClient } from '@azure/ai-projects';

export class AgentApiClient {
  private static client = new AIProjectsClient(
    process.env.FOUNDARY_CONNECTION_STRING!,
    new DefaultAzureCredential()
  );

  public static async sendPrompt(prompt: string): Promise<string> {
    const thread = await this.client.agents.createThread();
    await this.client.agents.createMessage(thread.id, { role: 'user', content: prompt });
    const run = await this.client.agents.createRun(thread.id, 'uicare-agent');
    if (run.status !== 'Succeeded') { throw new Error(run.lastError); }
    const messages = await this.client.agents.listMessages(thread.id);
    return messages.data.pop()!.content[0].text.value;
  }
}
```

### 5.7 src/extension/src/services/AuthService.ts
```ts
import * as vscode from 'vscode';
import { DeviceCodeCredential } from '@azure/identity';

export class AuthService {
  private static credential = new DeviceCodeCredential({
    tenantId: process.env.AZURE_TENANT_ID!,
    clientId: process.env.AZURE_CLIENT_ID!
  });

  public static async getToken(): Promise<string> {
    const token = await this.credential.getToken('https://management.azure.com/.default');
    return token.token;
  }
}
```

### 5.8 src/extension/src/views/ChatView.ts
```ts
import * as vscode from 'vscode';

export class ChatView {
  private static panel: vscode.WebviewPanel | undefined;

  public static showResponse(response: string) {
    if (!this.panel) {
      this.panel = vscode.window.createWebviewPanel(
        'uicareChat', 'UICare Chat', vscode.ViewColumn.Beside, {}
      );
    }
    this.panel.webview.html = `<html><body><pre>${response}</pre></body></html>`;
  }
}
```

### 5.9 src/extension/README.md
```markdown
# UICare VS Code Extension

## Setup
1. Install dependencies: `npm install`
2. Build: `npm run compile`
3. Open in VS Code and press F5 to launch Extension Development Host.

## Usage
- Press `Ctrl+Shift+P` and execute `UICare: Ask Agent` to prompt the AI.

## Configuration
- Set environment variable `FOUNDARY_CONNECTION_STRING` to your Azure Foundry connection string.
```

With this scaffold, you can immediately implement and test the UICare extension’s core functionality. Proceed to fill out remaining modules, then integrate with the orchestrator and backend.
